reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a series of operations:
    1. Transposed 3D convolution
    2. Mean pooling (across depth)
    3. Addition
    4. Softmax (across channels)
    5. Tanh activation
    6. Scaling
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, scaling_factor):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding)
        self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, out_channels, 1, 1, 1))  # Broadcastable bias over channels
        self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)                            # (B, C, D, H, W)
        x = x.mean(dim=2, keepdim=True)                       # Mean pool over depth dim (D)
        x = x + self.bias                                     # Bias add per channel
        x = torch.softmax(x, dim=1)                           # Softmax over channels
        x = torch.tanh(x)                                     # Nonlinearity
        x = x * self.scaling_factor                           # Scaling
        return x

# === Test config ===
batch_size = 16
in_channels  = 16  
out_channels = 64  
depth = 32; height = width = 128  
kernel_size  = 3
stride       = 1  
padding = 1
scaling_factor = 2.0

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, scaling_factor]

Let me analyze the reference implementation to understand the workload: 1. `ConvTranspose3d`: (B, in_channels, D, H, W) -> (B, out_channels, D, H, W) 2. Scale by constant The biggest opportunity for speedup is fusing steps 2-6 and possibly step 1. However, transposed convolutions are complex. We might be able to keep the ConvTranspose3d but fuse all subsequent operations into a single CUDA kernel. Let me check the ConvTranspose3d output shape: - Input: (16, 16, 32, 128, 128) - ConvTranspose3d with in_channels=16, out_channels=64, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1 - Output: (16, 64, 32, 128, 128) Then mean over dim=2 gives (16, 64, 1, 128, 128) Then add bias, softmax, tanh, scale. The key insight is that we can fuse the mean pooling + bias add + softmax + tanh + scaling into one kernel. This saves memory bandwidth for reading/writing the large tensor multiple times. Mean pooling over depth D=32: - For each (b, c, h, w), we average x[b, c, :, h, w] across depth. After mean, the result is (B, C, 1, H, W). Then bias add, softmax, tanh, scale. The sum over d of y[d] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} W[...] * sum_{d=0}^{31} x_padded[d + kd - 1] The inner sum sum_{d=0}^{31} x_padded[d + kd - 1] is: - For kd=0: sum_{d=0}^{31} x_padded[d - 1] = sum_{d=-1}^{30} x_padded[d] = x_padded[-1] + x[0] + ... + x[29] But x_padded[-1] = 0. So the sums are missing boundary elements! This means we CANNOT simply do a conv3d on mean-pooled input. The mean over depth commutes with the conv only if there is no padding, but here there is padding=1, so the boundary effects matter. This makes sense: we can't fuse the depth mean into the conv as a pre-processing step because of padding. For the spatial dims (h, w), we don't mean over them, so that's fine. Let me analyze the reference model: 1. `ConvTranspose3d` - This is the expensive part. Input shape: (16, 16, 32, 128, 128), output after conv transpose with kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=1 will be same spatial size: (16, 64, 32, 128, 128) 2. Mean pooling across depth (dim=2): (16, 64, 1, 128, 128) 3. Add bias: (16, 64, 1, 128, 128) 4. Softmax over channels (dim=1): (16, 64, 1, 128, 128) 5. Tanh: (16, 64, 1, 128, 128) 6. Scale: (16, 64, 1, 128, 128) The key insight is that after mean pooling, depth dimension becomes 1. The expensive part is ConvTranspose3d producing (B, C_out, D, H, W) and then mean-reducing over D. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=1, kernel_size=3, it's essentially a regular 3D convolution (transposed conv with stride=1 and padding=1 produces same output size as input when kernel=3, stride=1, padding=1). Actually for conv transpose: output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + kernel_size + output_padding. With stride=1, padding=1, kernel=3: output = (32-1)*1 - 2*1 + 3 = 32. Same for H,W. So output is (16, 64, 32, 128, 128). Then mean over D reduces to (16, 64, 1, 128, 128). The naive approach would be to just fuse all operations after conv, but we still need the conv. However, we can notice that mean over D of the conv output is equivalent to a modified convolution where each output pixel at (d, h, w) gets averaged over d. But since it's a mean over depth dimension, we can integrate it into the conv itself. For output at channel c, spatial (h, w), the mean over depth is: mean_d sum_{cin, kd, kh, kw} x[b, cin, d+kd-1, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * weight[cin, c, kd, kh, kw] Wait, conv transpose formula. Let me think more carefully. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=1, the output dimension is same as input. The operation is essentially: out[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} in[b, c_in, d + kd - 1, h + kh - 1, w + kw - 1] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] with appropriate padding handling (zeros outside). Then mean over d: mean_d out[b, c_out, d, h, w] = (1/D) * sum_d out[b, c_out, d, h, w] So the fused operation for mean over d would be: result[b, c_out, 0, h, w] = (1/D) * sum_d sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} in[b, c_in, d + kd - 1, h + kh - 1, w + kw - 1] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] We can swap sums: = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} ( (1/D) * sum_d in[b, c_in, d + kd - 1, h + kh - 1, w + kw - 1] ) * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] Notice that sum_d in[..., d + kd - 1, ...] is summing over depth with a shift. Since we sum over all valid d, and assuming zero padding, this is just summing the entire depth dimension but shifted by kd-1. Wait, but the depth dimension after padding should have size D + 2*padding (with padding=1, that's D+2=34). And the sum over d from 0 to D-1 of in[..., d + kd - 1, ...] gives different ranges. For kd=0: sum over in[..., -1, ...] (which is 0) to in[..., 0, ...] to in[..., D-2, ...]. Actually, let's think about this differently. If we depth-pad the input with zeros (padding=1), then the padded depth has indices -1 to D. Sum over d of padded_input[b, c_in, d + kd, h + kh, w + kw] for d=0..D-1 gives: - kd=0: sum over padded positions 0..D-1 - kd=1: sum over padded positions 1..D - kd=2: sum over padded positions 2..D+1 But since padding is 1 on each side, padded_input at -1 and D is 0. So: - kd=0: sum of input at positions 0..D-1 = sum over all depth - kd=1: sum of input at positions 1..D (plus padded D which is 0) = sum over all depth minus input at 0 - kd=2: sum of input at positions 2..D+1 (padded) = sum over all depth minus input at 0 and 1 Hmm, so it's not a simple uniform sum. For conv transpose: output[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} padded_in[b, c_in, d+kd-1, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] Then mean over d: out_mean[b, c_out, h, w] = (1/D) sum_d output[b, c_out, d, h, w] = (1/D) sum_d sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} padded_in[b, c_in, d+kd-1, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} sum_{kd} w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] * (1/D) sum_d padded_in[b, c_in, d+kd-1, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] Now focus on (1/D) sum_d padded_in[b, c_in, d+kd-1, h', w'] where h'=h+kh-1, w'=w+kw-1. For a fixed h', w', and for kd=0: (1/D) sum_{d=0}^{D-1} padded_in[b, c_in, d-1, h', w'] = (1/D) sum_{d=0}^{D-1} (d-1 >= 0 && d-1 < D ? in[b, c_in, d-1, h', w'] : 0) = (1/D) sum_{d=1}^{D-1} in[b, c_in, d-1, h', w'] (rewriting with d' = d-1...) = (1/D) sum_{d'=0}^{D-2} in[b, c_in, d', h', w'] = mean[b, c_in, h', w'] - (1/D) * in[b, c_in, D-1, h', w'] Wait, let me recompute: mean = (1/D) sum_{d'=0}^{D-1} in[b, c_in, d', h', w'] So sum_{d'=0}^{D-2} in[...] = D * mean - in[D-1] Therefore: (1/D) sum_{d'=0}^{D-2} in[...] = mean - in[D-1]/D For kd=1: (1/D) sum_{d=0}^{D-1} padded_in[b, c_in, d, h', w'] = (1/D) sum_{d=0}^{D-1} in[b, c_in, d, h', w'] = mean For kd=2: (1/D) sum_{d=0}^{D-1} padded_in[b, c_in, d+1, h', w'] = (1/D) sum_{d=0}^{D-2} in[b, c_in, d+1, h', w'] (since d+1 < D, i.e., d < D-1) = (1/D) sum_{d'=1}^{D-1} in[b, c_in, d', h', w'] (d' = d+1) = mean - in[0]/D So: sum_{kd} w_kd * s_kd = w0 * (mean - in[D-1]/D) + w1 * mean + w2 * (mean - in[0]/D) = (w0 + w1 + w2) * mean - w0 * in[D-1]/D - w2 * in[0]/D So my earlier formula had w0 and w2 swapped for the boundary terms, but the structure is correct. Then: out_mean[b, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} (w0 + w1 + w2)[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] * mean[b, c_in, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] - (1/D) * sum_{c_in, kh, kw} w0[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] * in[D-1][b, c_in, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] - (1/D) * sum_{c_in, kh, kw} w2[c_in, c_out, kh, kw] * in[0][b, c_in, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] This is: = Conv2d(mean_input, w_sum) - (1/D) * Conv2d(input_last, w0) - (1/D) * Conv2d(input_first, w2) Where w_sum[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] = w0 + w1 + w2 (but wait, PyTorch conv2d uses [out_ch, in_ch, ...], while our conv_transpose3d weight is [in_ch, out_ch, ...] because conv_transpose!) Let me check PyTorch weight shapes: - ConvTranspose3d weight shape: (in_channels, out_channels, kd, kh, kw) - Conv2d weight shape: (out_channels, in_channels, kh, kw) For ConvTranspose3d: output[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kd, kh, kw} input[b, c_in, d+kd-1, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] For Conv2d: output[b, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kh, kw} input[b, c_in, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * weight[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] So to express our formula with Conv2d: out_mean = Conv2d(mean_input, W_sum^T) - (1/D) * Conv2d(input_last, W_0^T) - (1/D) * Conv2d(input_first, W_2^T) Where: - W_sum[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] = weight[c_in, c_out, 0, kh, kw] + weight[c_in, c_out, 1, kh, kw] + weight[c_in, c_out, 2, kh, kw] - W_0[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] = weight[c_in, c_out, 0, kh, kw] - W_2[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] = weight[c_in, c_out, 2, kh, kw] And mean_input[b, c_in, h, w] = (1/D) * sum_d input[b, c_in, d, h, w] input_first[b, c_in, h, w] = input[b, c_in, 0, h, w] input_last[b, c_in, h, w] = input[b, c_in, D-1, h, w] This reduces the problem from ~232B MACs to ~7.2B MACs, a 32x reduction! The extra operations are: - mean over depth: B*C_in*D*H*W adds = 16*16*32*128*128 ≈ 134M ops, negligible - 3 conv2d ops using highly optimized cuDNN After the conv2d operations, we get (B, C_out, H, W). We need to add bias (over channels), then softmax over channels, tanh, and scale. We can fuse: bias add + softmax + tanh + scale into a single kernel. Actually, let's verify this is faster. The original is one conv_transpose3d (~232B MACs) + mean + bias + softmax + tanh + scale. The new approach is mean (134M ops) + 3 conv2d (~7.2B MACs) + fused bias/softmax/tanh/scale. 3 conv2d at 7.2B MACs vs 1 conv_transpose3d at 232B MACs. Even if conv2d is slightly less efficient per MAC than conv3d (which it might not be - 2D conv is very well optimized), it's still a massive win. But can we do even better? The 3 conv2d can potentially be batched together. Or, we can notice that: Conv2d(mean_input, W_sum) - (1/D)*Conv2d(input_last, W_0) - (1/D)*Conv2d(input_first, W_2) = Conv2d(mean_input, W_sum) + Conv2d(-input_last/D, W_0) + Conv2d(-input_first/D, W_2) We could concatenate inputs and weights: input_cat = [mean_input, -input_last/D, -input_first/D] -> (B, 3*C_in, H, W) weight_cat = [W_sum, W_0, W_2] but these need to be applied to different input channel groups. Actually, in grouped conv with groups=3: input: (B, 3*C_in, H, W) weight: (3*C_out, C_in, 3, 3) with groups=3 This computes 3 separate conv2d operations in one call! And cuDNN is very good at grouped conv. Let me verify: - Group 0: input channels [0:C_in], weight channels [0:C_out], output channels [0:C_out] - Group 1: input channels [C_in:2*C_in], weight channels [C_out:2*C_out], output channels [C_out:2*C_out] - Group 2: input channels [2*C_in:3*C_in], weight channels [2*C_out:3*C_out], output channels [2*C_out:3*C_out] Then output_cat = [out0, out1, out2] where: out0 = Conv2d(mean_input, W_sum) out1 = Conv2d(-input_last/D, W_0) out2 = Conv2d(-input_first/D, W_2) Then final = out0 + out1 + out2 (sum over the 3 channel groups). This is elegant! We do: 1. Compute mean_input, neg_input_last/D, neg_input_first/D 2. Concatenate along channel dim 3. Single grouped conv2d with groups=3 4. Sum the 3 output groups Even better, steps 1 and 2 can be done with simple tensor operations. Step 4 is a simple sum. Let me code this up! Original conv_transpose3d weight: (in_channels, out_channels, kd, kh, kw) = (16, 64, 3, 3, 3) For Conv2d, weight should be (out_channels, in_channels, kh, kw). W_sum for conv2d: shape (64, 16, 3, 3), where W_sum[c_out, c_in, kh, kw] = weight[c_in, c_out, 0, kh, kw] + weight[c_in, c_out, 1, kh, kw] + weight[c_in, c_out, 2, kh, kw] In PyTorch: ```python # weight is (16, 64, 3, 3, 3) w_sum = weight.sum(dim=2).permute(1, 0, 2, 3) # (64, 16, 3, 3) w_0 = weight[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) # (64, 16, 3, 3) w_2 = weight[:, :, 2, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) # (64, 16, 3, 3) ``` Inputs for grouped conv: - mean_input shape: (B, 16, H, W) - neg_last = -input[:, :, -1, :, :] / D shape: (B, 16, H, W) - neg_first = -input[:, :, 0, :, :] / D shape: (B, 16, H, W) Concatenated: (B, 48, H, W) Weights for grouped conv: - Concatenate W_sum, W_0, W_2 along out_channels: (192, 16, 3, 3) - groups=3 This conv2d output: (B, 192, H, W) - out[:, 0:64, :, :] = Conv2d(mean_input, W_sum) - out[:, 64:128, :, :] = Conv2d(neg_last, W_0) - out[:, 128:192, :, :] = Conv2d(neg_first, W_2) Result: sum over these three channel groups = out[:, 0:64] + out[:, 64:128] + out[:, 128:192] Then add bias (which is (1, 64, 1, 1, 1) in original, so we need to squeeze or reshape to (1, 64, 1, 1) for the 4D tensor). Let me check how much error that introduces. The boundary terms are: -(1/D) * Conv2d(input_last, W_0) - (1/D) * Conv2d(input_first, W_2) For D=32, the factor is 1/32. If the weights and inputs are O(1), then each boundary term is O(1/32) of a conv2d output. The total error from ignoring them would be O(1/16) of the output magnitude. With fp32 tolerance 1e-4 relative, and if outputs are ~1, the absolute tolerance is 1e-4. But an O(1/16) error is ~0.06, which is way larger than 1e-4. So we absolutely CANNOT ignore the boundary terms. We must include them. Now, for the softmax: softmax is sensitive to the absolute values. Even a small error could change the softmax output. The standard relationship: conv_transpose is the adjoint of conv. If conv with kernel K computes y = K * x (with some padding), then conv_transpose with kernel K computes the operation that, when backpropagated through conv, multiplies by K^T. For stride=1 conv with padding=p, the forward pass is: y[i] = sum_k K[k] * x[i + k - p] The transpose (gradient w.r.t. x) is: dx[i] = sum_k K[k] * dy[i - k + p] This is equivalent to convolving dy with K flipped, with some padding. From PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose1d: "Applying ConvTranspose1d is the opposite of Conv1d. Conv1d reduces the spatial dimensions, while ConvTranspose1d increases them." And specifically, for ConvTranspose1d with stride=1, padding=p: The relationship with Conv1d: If you have Conv1d(in=3, out=1, kernel=3, stride=1, padding=1), and ConvTranspose1d(in=1, out=3, kernel=3, stride=1, padding=1), they are "adjoint" operations. For Conv1d: out[n] = sum_k W[k] * in[n + k - p] For ConvTranspose1d: out[n] = sum_k W[k] * in[n - k + p] ? If we think of Conv1d as a matrix multiplication where each row corresponds to an output element and each column to an input element, then ConvTranspose1d uses the transpose of that matrix. For Conv1d with input length L, output length L (stride=1, padding=1, kernel=3): Row for output n has nonzeros at columns n-1+p, n+p, n+1+p, i.e., n, n+1, n+2 for p=1? Wait. Actually, for Conv1d padding=1, stride=1, kernel=3: out[n] = w[0]*in[n-1] + w[1]*in[n] + w[2]*in[n+1] where in[-1] and in[L] are padded to 0. So row n of the matrix has w[0] at column n-1, w[1] at column n, w[2] at column n+1. The transpose matrix has column n with w[0] at row n-1, w[1] at row n, w[2] at row n+1. So for ConvTranspose1d: out[m] = w[0]*in[m+1] + w[1]*in[m] + w[2]*in[m-1] = w[2]*in[m-1] + w[1]*in[m] + w[0]*in[m+1] For m=0: out[0] = w[2]*in[-1] + w[1]*in[0] + w[0]*in[1] = w[1]*in[0] + w[0]*in[1] For m=L-1: out[L-1] = w[2]*in[L-2] + w[1]*in[L-1] + w[0]*in[L] = w[2]*in[L-2] + w[1]*in[L-1] This matches what I got in the second derivation if we identify W[k] -> w[k] and the formula is: out[m] = sum_k w[k] * in[m + 1 - k] = w[0]*in[m+1] + w[1]*in[m] + w[2]*in[m-1] But this is with the weight REFLECTED/FLIPPED compared to what I first thought. In my first derivation, I had out[m] = w[0]*in[m-1] + w[1]*in[m] + w[2]*in[m+1], which is the same as conv, not conv transpose. Wait, but in PyTorch's ConvTranspose, does the weight get flipped automatically during the operation? Yes! The operation itself applies the flipped kernel. So the actual formula for PyTorch ConvTranspose1d is: out[n] = sum_k weight[k] * in[n + padding - k * dilation] For padding=1, dilation=1, kernel=3: out[n] = weight[0]*in[n+1] + weight[1]*in[n] + weight[2]*in[n-1] This is equivalent to correlation with the unflipped weight, or convolution with the flipped weight. So my second derivation was correct: out[d] = W[0]*in[d+1] + W[1]*in[d] + W[2]*in[d-1] = W[0]*in[d+1] + W[1]*in[d] + W[2]*in[d-1] And the mean gives: mean = (W[0] + W[1] + W[2]) * mean_input - W[0]*in[-1]/D - W[2]*in[0]/D Wait, earlier I got: mean = (W[0] + W[1] + W[2]) * mean_input - W[0]*in[0]/D - W[2]*in[D-1]/D And just now I got: mean = (W[0] + W[1] + W[2]) * mean_input - W[0]*in[D-1]/D - W[2]*in[0]/D Which is correct? Let me recompute more carefully using the second formula: out[d] = W[0]*in[d+1] + W[1]*in[d] + W[2]*in[d-1] For d=0: out[0] = W[0]*in[1] + W[1]*in[0] + W[2]*0 = W[0]*in[1] + W[1]*in[0] For d=1: out[1] = W[0]*in[2] + W[1]*in[1] + W[2]*in[0] For d=D-2: out[D-2] = W[0]*in[D-1] + W[1]*in[D-2] + W[2]*in[D-3] For d=D-1: out[D-1] = W[0]*0 + W[1]*in[D-1] + W[2]*in[D-2] = W[1]*in[D-1] + W[2]*in[D-2] Sum over d: in[0]: appears in out[0] with coeff W[1], out[1] with coeff W[2] Total: W[1] + W[2] in[1]: appears in out[0] (W[0]), out[1] (W[1]), out[2] (W[2]) Total: W[0] + W[1] + W[2] in[i] for 2 <= i <= D-3: appears in out[i-1] (W[0]), out[i] (W[1]), out[i+1] (W[2]) Total: W[0] + W[1] + W[2] in[D-2]: appears in out[D-3] (W[0]), out[D-2] (W[1]), out[D-1] (W[2]) Total: W[0] + W[1] + W[2] in[D-1]: appears in out[D-2] (W[0]), out[D-1] (W[1]) Total: W[0] + W[1] Mean: (1/D) * [ in[0]*(W[1]+W[2]) + sum_{i=1}^{D-2} in[i]*(W[0]+W[1]+W[2]) + in[D-1]*(W[0]+W[1]) ] = (W[0]+W[1]+W[2]) * mean_input + in[0]*(W[1]+W[2] - (W[0]+W[1]+W[2]))/D + in[D-1]*(W[0]+W[1] - (W[0]+W[1]+W[2]))/D = (W[0]+W[1]+W[2]) * mean_input - in[0]*W[0]/D - in[D-1]*W[2]/D So the boundary terms are: - W[0] * in[0] / D (first slice) - W[2] * in[D-1] / D (last slice) Where W[0] and W[2] refer to the depth dimension kernels at kd=0 and kd=2. And W_sum = W[0] + W[1] + W[2] (sum over depth dimension of the weight). This is correct. Now, in PyTorch, the weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels, kd, kh, kw). So: - W[0] = weight[:, :, 0, :, :] - W[1] = weight[:, :, 1, :, :] - W[2] = weight[:, :, 2, :, :] For Conv2d, the weight needs to be (out_channels, in_channels, kh, kw). So: - W_sum_conv2d = (weight[:, :, 0, :, :] + weight[:, :, 1, :, :] + weight[:, :, 2, :, :]).permute(1, 0, 2, 3) - W_0_conv2d = weight[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) - W_2_conv2d = weight[:, :, 2, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) The input terms: - mean_input = x.mean(dim=2) # shape (B, C_in, H, W) - first_slice = x[:, :, 0, :, :] # shape (B, C_in, H, W) - last_slice = x[:, :, -1, :, :] # shape (B, C_in, H, W) Then: result = F.conv2d(mean_input, W_sum_conv2d, padding=1) \ - F.conv2d(first_slice, W_0_conv2d, padding=1) / D \ - F.conv2d(last_slice, W_2_conv2d, padding=1) / D Actually, I can factor out the /D from the last two terms: result = conv2d(mean_input, W_sum) - conv2d(first_slice, W_0) / D - conv2d(last_slice, W_2) / D Or combine into single conv: concat_input = torch.cat([mean_input, -first_slice/D, -last_slice/D], dim=1) # (B, 48, H, W) concat_weight = torch.cat([W_sum, W_0, W_2], dim=1) # Wait, no. For a standard conv2d: output[b, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in} input[..., c_in, ...] * weight[c_out, c_in, ...] If input has 48 channels and weight has shape (64, 48, 3, 3): output = sum_{c_in=0}^{47} input[..., c_in, ...] * weight[..., c_out, c_in, ...] If we want this to equal: sum_{c_in=0}^{15} mean_input[..., c_in, ...] * W_sum[c_out, c_in, ...] + sum_{c_in=0}^{15} (-first_slice[..., c_in, ...]/D) * W_0[c_out, c_in, ...] + sum_{c_in=0}^{15} (-last_slice[..., c_in, ...]/D) * W_2[c_out, c_in, ...] Then we set: weight[c_out, 0:16, :, :] = W_sum[c_out, :, :, :] weight[c_out, 16:32, :, :] = W_0[c_out, :, :, :] weight[c_out, 32:48, :, :] = W_2[c_out, :, :, :] And input = [mean_input, -first_slice/D, -last_slice/D] Then output = Conv2d(input, weight, padding=1) This gives the exact same result with a single conv2d call! After the conv2d, we add bias (squeeze from 5D to match 4D: (1, 64, 1, 1)), then softmax, tanh, scale, and finally unsqueeze to get depth dim back: (B, C_out, 1, H, W). Yes, because we're using padding=1 just like the spatial dimensions of the original conv_transpose. Another concern: numerical precision. We're doing the exact same mathematical operations, just reorganized. There should be no precision loss relative to doing conv3d then mean, provided conv2d and conv3d have similar numerical properties (which they do, both use fp32 accumulation). However, there might be small differences due to: 1. Different order of operations (conv2d might use different tiling/accumulation order than conv3d) 2. The mean in the reference is computed after conv, while in our version it's computed before conv. These could lead to tiny numerical differences due to floating point non-associativity. The tolerance is 1e-4 relative and absolute. For values that are O(1), this means differences up to 1e-4 are acceptable. The difference due to operation ordering should be around machine epsilon * sqrt(N) where N is the number of terms. For N=32 (depth), that's about 1e-7 * 6 ≈ 6e-7. For conv accumulation with 16*9=144 terms, it's about 1e-7 * 12 ≈ 1e-6. Combined maybe a few e-6. That's well below 1e-4. Wait, but in our case, the reference does conv3d with 16*27=432 MACs per output element, then mean over 32 values. Our approach does mean over 32 values first, then conv2d with 48*9=432 MACs per output element. The total number of MACs is the same (actually we reduced it by avoiding the full 3D output). But the accumulation order is completely different. Could the difference exceed 1e-4? For standard normal inputs and weights, the conv3d output might be O(sqrt(N)) ≈ O(20) in magnitude if inputs/weights are standard normal. The rounding error in conv3d with 432 MACs is about 20 * 1e-7 * sqrt(432) ≈ 4e-5. Then mean adds error of about 20 * 1e-7 * sqrt(32) ≈ 1e-5. Total maybe ~5e-5. Our approach: mean of 32 values has error ~1 * 1e-7 * sqrt(32) ≈ 6e-7. Then conv2d with 432 MACs on inputs that are O(1) (because they're means) has error ~1 * 1e-7 * sqrt(432) ≈ 2e-6. The difference between the two approaches could be of the same order as the individual errors, maybe up to ~1e-4 in worst case for specific inputs. But for random inputs, it should be much smaller. Let me write a clean kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_postprocess( const float* __restrict__ input, // (B, C, H, W) const float* __restrict__ bias, // (C,) float* __restrict__ output, // (B, C, 1, H, W) int B, int C, int H, int W, float scaling_factor ) { // Each block handles one (b, h, w) position int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; // Base index for this (b, h, w) in input int base_idx = ((b * C) * H + h) * W + w; // Load all channels for this position into shared memory extern __shared__ float sdata[]; // Each thread handles a subset of channels int tid = threadIdx.x; int nthreads = blockDim.x; // First, find max float max_val = -1e30f; for (int c = tid; c < C; c += nthreads) { float val = input[base_idx + c * H * W] + bias[c]; sdata[c] = val; max_val = fmaxf(max_val, val); } // Block-level reduction for max // ... using warp shuffle and shared mem __syncthreads(); // Compute exp and sum float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = tid; c < C; c += nthreads) { float val = expf(sdata[c] - max_val); sdata[c] = val; sum_exp += val; } // Block-level reduction for sum __syncthreads(); // Normalize, apply tanh, scale, and write to output float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; int out_base = (((b * C + 0) * 1 + h) * W + w); // Actually output is (B, C, 1, H, W) // For (B, C, D, H, W) with D=1: idx = ((b * C + c) * 1 + h) * W + w = (b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w for (int c = tid; c < C; c += nthreads) { float val = sdata[c] * inv_sum; val = tanhf(val); val *= scaling_factor; int out_idx = ((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w; output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` Wait, if I use 1 block per (b, h, w), and blockDim.x = say 128, but C = 64. I'll create: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, scaling_factor): super().__init__() # Keep original layer for weight compatibility self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding) self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, out_channels, 1, 1, 1)) self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor # Precompute derived conv2d weights as buffers with torch.no_grad(): w = self.conv_transpose.weight # (in, out, 3, 3, 3) # For PyTorch ConvTranspose3d, the operation corresponds to: # out[d] = sum_k w[:, :, k, ...] * in[d + 1 - k] with zero padding # Based on my derivation: # result = conv2d(mean, W_sum) - conv2d(first/D, W_0) - conv2d(last/D, W_2) # where W_* are (out, in, 3, 3) w_0 = w[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3).contiguous() # (out, in, 3, 3) w_1 = w[:, :, 1, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3).contiguous() w_2 = w[:, :, 2, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3).contiguous() w_sum = (w_0 + w_1 + w_2).contiguous() # Combined weight for single conv2d: (out, 3*in, 3, 3) self.register_buffer('conv2d_weight', torch.cat([w_sum, w_0, w_2], dim=1)) # Combined bias: conv_transpose.bias + self.bias if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: self.register_buffer('total_bias', (self.conv_transpose.bias.view(1, -1, 1, 1) + self.bias.squeeze()).contiguous()) else: self.register_buffer('total_bias', self.bias.squeeze().contiguous()) ``` Wait, the `total_bias` depends on `self.bias` which is a Parameter. Using `register_buffer` for `total_bias` means it won't be updated during training if `self.bias` changes. But for inference (forward pass), it's fine if we compute it on the fly or if we assume weights don't change. For the benchmark, weights are fixed, so we can compute it in __init__ or forward. But if we register it as a buffer using .data, it won't track gradients for `self.bias`. That's fine for inference. Actually, an even simpler approach: just don't register precomputed weights and compute everything in forward. The overhead is tiny. In forward: ```python def forward(self, x): B, C, D, H, W = x.shape # Compute the three depth-reduced inputs mean_input = x.mean(dim=2) # (B, C, H, W) first_slice = x[:, :, 0, :, :] # (B, C, H, W) last_slice = x[:, :, -1, :, :] # (B, C, H, W) # Negate and scale boundary terms neg_first = -first_slice / D neg_last = -last_slice / D # Concatenate combined_input = torch.cat([mean_input, neg_first, neg_last], dim=1) # (B, 3*C, H, W) # Prepare weight w = self.conv_transpose.weight # (C_in, C_out, 3, 3, 3) w_0 = w[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) # (C_out, C_in, 3, 3) w_1 = w[:, :, 1, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) w_2 = w[:, :, 2, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) w_sum = w_0 + w_1 + w_2 conv2d_weight = torch.cat([w_sum, w_0, w_2], dim=1) # (C_out, 3*C_in, 3, 3) # Compute conv2d conv_out = F.conv2d(combined_input, conv2d_weight, padding=1) # (B, C_out, H, W) # Add combined bias if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: total_bias = self.conv_transpose.bias + self.bias.view(-1) else: total_bias = self.bias.view(-1) conv_out = conv_out + total_bias.view(1, -1, 1, 1) # Now apply softmax, tanh, scale, and reshape # We can use a custom kernel here, or fuse with PyTorch ops # For now, let's use our custom kernel # ... custom CUDA call ... ``` Wait, I need to be very careful about the bias shapes. - `self.conv_transpose.bias` shape: (out_channels,) - `self.bias` shape: (1, out_channels, 1, 1, 1) - `total_bias` should be (out_channels,) or broadcastable to (B, out_channels, H, W) `self.conv_transpose.bias + self.bias.view(out_channels)` gives (out_channels,). Now, for the custom kernel, the input is (B, C_out, H, W), and output should be (B, C_out, 1, H, W). Let me write the actual CUDA kernel for the post-processing. I'll use the approach where each block handles one (b, h, w) and uses shared memory for the C_out=64 channels. Actually, let me write a kernel that: - Uses 1 block per (b, h, w) - 64 threads per block - Shared memory for 64 floats - Warp-level reductions for softmax But 64 threads is only 2 warps. We can still do this efficiently. ```cuda __global__ void fused_postprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, float scaling_factor ) { // Block = one (b, h, w) int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; int base = ((b * C) * H + h) * W + w; // Step 1: add bias and find max float max_val = -1e30f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float val = input[base + c * H * W] + bias[c]; max_val = fmaxf(max_val, val); } // Warp reduce max #pragma unroll for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, offset)); } // Broadcast max to all threads in warp max_val = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, 0); // Step 2: compute exp and sum float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float val = expf(input[base + c * H * W] + bias[c] - max_val); sum_exp += val; } // Warp reduce sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 32; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, offset); } sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); // Step 3: normalize, tanh, scale, write float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < C; c += blockDim.x) { float val = expf(input[base + c * H * W] + bias[c] - max_val) * inv_sum; val = tanhf(val); val *= scaling_factor; // Output layout: (B, C, 1, H, W) int out_idx = ((b * C + c) * 1 + h) * W + w; // Actually for 5D tensor (B, C, D, H, W), contiguous memory layout is // index = (((b * C + c) * D + d) * H + h) * W + w // With D=1: index = ((b * C + c) * 1 + h) * W + w = (b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w // Wait, let me double check. // For shape (B, C, D, H, W), stride is (C*D*H*W, D*H*W, H*W, W, 1) // So idx = b*C*D*H*W + c*D*H*W + d*H*W + h*W + w // With D=1, d=0: idx = (b*C + c) * H * W + h * W + w int out_idx2 = (b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx2] = val; } } ``` Wait, each thread loops over channels with step blockDim.x. If blockDim.x = 64 and C = 64, each thread handles exactly 1 channel. Perfect! With 64 threads per block and C=64, there's no loop needed. And we have 262k blocks of 64 threads. Total 16.7M threads. On H100, each SM can handle up to 2048 threads, so we have ~8200 thread blocks per SM concurrently (if 32 blocks/SM * 64 threads). With 132 SMs, total concurrent blocks = 4224. But we have 262k blocks, so it's ~62 waves. Wait, 32 blocks per SM * 132 SMs = 4224 blocks concurrently. 262k / 4224 ≈ 62 waves. Each wave processes 4224 blocks, each block reads 64 floats (256 bytes) and writes 64 floats. Total memory per wave: 4224 * 512 bytes = 2.1MB. Wait no, each block processes one (b, h, w), reading C=64 channels = 256 bytes. Writing one position = 256 bytes. So total per block = 512 bytes. Per wave = 4224 * 512 = 2.16MB. At 3.35 TB/s, a wave takes ~0.64us. 62 waves = ~40us. Plus some compute overhead. Total maybe ~50us. This is very fast and reasonable. But block overhead for 62 waves on 132 SMs might be non-trivial. Each wave launch takes some time. But 62 waves is not too many. Maybe total kernel time is ~80-100us. Let me also consider: can we reduce the number of blocks by having each block handle multiple positions? Yes, maybe 4 positions per block with 256 threads. That would reduce waves to ~16. But the kernel is memory-bound, so the actual execution time might not change much. For simplicity, I'll go with 1 block per (b, h, w) and 64 threads. One concern: `__shfl_sync` and `__shfl_down_sync` require the threads to be in the same warp. With 64 threads per block (2 warps), the warp shuffle only works within a warp! So I need to either: 1. Use shared memory for cross-warp reduction, OR 2. Ensure all threads in a block are in one warp, which means max 32 threads, OR 3. Use `blockDim.x <= 32` Option 3 is simplest. Use 32 threads per block, each handling 2 channels. Then all threads are in one warp, and warp shuffle works for the entire block. We need a loop. ```cuda __global__ void kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; int tid = threadIdx.x; int base = ((b * C) * H + h) * W + w; float max_val = -1e30f; for (int c = tid; c < C; c += 32) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, input[base + c * H * W] + bias[c]); } // Warp reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, offset)); } max_val = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, 0); float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = tid; c < C; c += 32) { sum_exp += expf(input[base + c * H * W] + bias[c] - max_val); } for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_exp += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, offset); } sum_exp = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, sum_exp, 0); float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int c = tid; c < C; c += 32) { float val = expf(input[base + c * H * W] + bias[c] - max_val) * inv_sum; val = tanhf(val) * scaling_factor; int out_idx = (b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` With 32 threads per block and 262k blocks, we have 8.4M threads. Total concurrent blocks = 4224 (as before, but now each block has 32 threads, so maybe 64 blocks per SM = 8448 concurrent blocks). 262k / 8448 ≈ 31 waves. Half the waves of the 64-thread version. Each wave reads/writes ~8448 * 32 * 8 bytes = 2.1MB. Same memory per wave. Total ~31 * 0.64us = 20us of pure memory time. But with overhead, maybe 30-50us. This is even better. Actually, which is better: 64 threads (2 warps) with shared memory reduction, or 32 threads (1 warp) with warp shuffle? For 64 threads with 2 warps, we'd use shared memory to combine the results of the two warps. That's a bit more code but perfectly fine. With 64 threads, each loop has 1 iteration (c = tid, since C=64 and blockDim.x=64, step=64 means each thread handles exactly 1 channel). This means less loop overhead and fewer memory accesses scattered by the loop. Wait, with C=64 and blockDim.x=64, the for loop `for (int c = tid; c < C; c += 64)` executes exactly once for each thread. If blockDim.x=32, the loop executes twice for each thread. So blockDim.x=64 has better memory access pattern if we ensure each warp accesses contiguous memory... Actually, let me think about memory coalescing. Threads in a warp access channels. The memory layout is (B, C, H, W). For a fixed (b, h, w), channel c is at offset c * H * W from the base of (b, 0, h, w). If thread 0 accesses c=0, thread 1 accesses c=1, etc., the addresses are c * H * W apart. Since H*W = 16384, consecutive threads access addresses 16384 * 4 = 64KB apart! That's NOT coalesced at all! Oh no! I completely forgot about this. In PyTorch's NCHW layout, adjacent channels are far apart in memory for a given spatial position. Consecutive threads accessing consecutive channels result in strided memory access with stride H*W, which is terrible for coalescing. For coalesced access, threads in a warp should access adjacent elements. Since the output for a given (b, c, h, w) is at offset ((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w, adjacent w positions are adjacent in memory. So for coalescing, we should have consecutive threads work on consecutive w positions! This means we should organize threads along the W dimension, not the C dimension. A much better kernel layout: - Each block handles a tile of (b, h, w_span) - Threads are organized along w - Each thread handles all channels for its w position - But softmax requires all channels for a single (b, h, w), so each thread does the softmax reduction independently or via shared memory Wait, if each thread handles all channels for one (b, h, w), and consecutive threads handle consecutive w, then: - Thread 0 accesses all channels for w=0 - Thread 1 accesses all channels for w=1 - These are adjacent in memory (different w, same c, h, b) For a fixed c, w=0 and w=1 are adjacent. But each thread needs ALL channels. So thread 0 accesses (c=0, w=0), then (c=1, w=0) which is H*W away. This is still strided. To make channel access coalesced, we need threads in a warp to access the same channel but different w. Like: - Thread 0: (c=0, w=0) - Thread 1: (c=0, w=1) - Thread 31: (c=0, w=31) Then all threads in warp access consecutive memory locations. Perfect coalescing! But then for softmax, each warp would compute the softmax for one channel across 32 w positions simultaneously, not for one (b, h, w) across all channels. Hmm, the natural parallelism for softmax is either: 1. Parallel over (b, h, w), serial over C - bad for coalescing with NCHW 2. Parallel over C for a single (b, h, w) - bad for coalescing with NCHW 3. Use a different approach Actually, for PyTorch's NCHW format, there is no way to have both channel-wise and spatial-wise parallelism coalesced simultaneously unless we transpose the data. For the post-processing kernel, the bottleneck is memory bandwidth. Even with strided access, a single warp can read at full bandwidth if the cache line is utilized. For H100 with 128-byte cache lines, reading 32 floats spaced 64KB apart brings in 32 cache lines. That's 32 * 128 bytes = 4KB. The warp reads 32 * 4 bytes = 128 bytes of useful data. Utilization is 128 / 4096 = 3%. That's terrible! So we definitely need to reorder access. One option is to have the kernel first transpose from NCHW to NHWC in shared memory, then do the channel-wise operations. Here's a better approach: - Block handles a 2D tile of spatial positions, say 8x8 - Also handles a subset of channels, say 8 channels per thread block iteration - Use shared memory to accumulate partial sums Actually, for softmax we need all channels. So if we tile over spatial positions and have all channels in shared memory... wait, 64 channels * 4 bytes = 256 bytes per position. For 8x8 = 64 positions, that's 16KB of shared memory. That's fine! But we need each spatial position to have all 64 channels accessible for softmax. If we store data in shared memory as [position][channel], then to load from global memory we need to read strided data, which is bad. Another approach: - Each block handles multiple spatial positions (say 16 positions = 4x4) - For loading, use all threads to read spatially coalesced data for one channel at a time - Store in shared memory as [channel][position] or transpose - Then each warp/thread handles one position's softmax over all channels using the transposed shared memory layout Let me think about this more concretely. Block: 256 threads. Handles: say 16 spatial positions (b, h, w) × 16 channels at a time? No. Actually, for C=64, we can process all channels for a group of positions. Let's say we process 4 positions at a time with 64 threads. Wait, a cleaner way: since C=64 is small, we can just use shared memory to transpose: 1. All threads in block (say 256 threads) cooperatively load input for 4 positions × 64 channels = 256 floats. - But loading 256 floats with 256 threads: each thread loads 1 float. - If we load for position 0 first (all 64 channels), that's 64 threads loading strided memory. Bad. 2. Better: load for channel 0 first, all positions. 4 positions = 4 floats per thread if each thread handles 1 position. - With 256 threads, we could load 256 positions for channel 0 in one go. But we only need 4 positions. Hmm, this is getting complicated for a kernel that's only ~50us anyway. Since the conv2d is ~0.1-0.2ms, even if our post-process kernel takes 200us, the total is still ~0.3-0.4ms, giving us 10-15x speedup. So for the first attempt, let me just write a simple kernel using PyTorch ops without a custom CUDA kernel for post-processing. Let me see if that's acceptable - I still need to have SOME custom CUDA kernel. Actually, I can write a very simple custom CUDA kernel that just reshapes or copies, and use PyTorch ops for everything else. But the instructions say to "replace the forward computation with a custom CUDA kernel", implying the custom kernel should do meaningful work. Or, I can write a custom kernel that does the entire post-processing, and accept that it might not be perfectly coalesced but is still very fast. Given that the reference takes ~4ms and our conv2d takes ~0.1ms, even a poorly written post-process kernel that takes 0.5ms would give us ~7x speedup. And 0.5ms to process 67MB is terrible bandwidth (134MB / 0.5ms = 268GB/s, way below 3.35TB/s). If the kernel takes 0.5ms due to poor coalescing, let me see: 67MB of output and 67MB of input strided reads. Strided reads mean we read ~32 cache lines per warp (one per channel for 32 threads). Cache line is 128 bytes. So for 32 threads reading 32 channels for one position, we bring in 32*128 = 4KB but only use 128 bytes. That's 32x data movement. So total memory moved = 32 * 67MB = 2.1GB for reading, plus 67MB for writing = 2.17GB. At 3.35TB/s, that's ~0.65ms. Hmm, that's still acceptable. Actually, 32x blowup is worst case if cache lines don't overlap. Since consecutive channels are H*W*4 = 64KB apart, and cache line is 128 bytes, there's no overlap. So yes, ~32x blowup. For C=64, it's 64 cache lines per position = 8KB brought in for 256 bytes used. That's still a 32x blowup for the read portion. So 2.1GB read + 67MB write = 2.17GB total. At 3.35TB/s = ~0.65ms. That's still fine for our purposes since the conv2d is only 0.1ms. But let me try to do better. We can use shared memory as a staging area. Here's the plan: For a block handling a (b, h) row and a range of w: 1. We need to compute softmax over 64 channels for each w. 2. We can load all channels for one w position into registers or shared memory. But loading strided is bad. 3. Alternative: load data in a coalesced way and transpose in shared memory. Block: 64 threads. Processes: let's say each block processes one (b, h) and 16 consecutive w positions, with each thread handling one channel and one w position? No... Let me try a different layout: - BlockDim.x = 64 (channels) - BlockDim.y = 4 (w positions) - Total threads = 256 - Grid: (B, H, W/4) For each block: - Process 4 consecutive w positions for one (b, h) - For loading channel c for all 4 w positions: - Thread (c, w_offset) loads input[b, c, h, w_base + w_offset] - This is coalesced because threads with same w_offset but consecutive c load... wait no. - Thread (c, 0) loads w_base + 0 at channel c - Thread (c, 1) loads w_base + 1 at channel c - Thread (c+1, 0) loads w_base + 0 at channel c+1 - These are 64KB apart! Not coalesced. For coalesced loading, we need consecutive threads to access consecutive memory. So: - Thread 0: (b, 0, h, w_base) - Thread 1: (b, 0, h, w_base + 1) - Thread 2: (b, 0, h, w_base + 2) - ... This accesses W dimension consecutively. Good! Actually, let's use 64 threads = one thread per spatial position in the tile. But each spatial position has 64 channels. So after loading, each thread processes all 64 channels for its position. For loading, we need 64 threads to load 8x8x64 = 4096 floats. That's 64 floats per thread. We can load in 64 iterations, one channel per iteration: ``` for c = 0 to 63: spatial_idx = threadIdx.x // 0..63 h = spatial_idx / 8 w = spatial_idx % 8 global_idx = ((b * C + c) * H + h_start + h) * W + w_start + w smem[c][spatial_idx] = input[global_idx] ``` For a fixed c, all 64 threads load their spatial position. For H100 shared memory, accessing 64 elements with 64-element stride from different threads... wait, each thread accesses its OWN column, so there's no bank conflict (each thread reads from a different bank because the bank size is 4 bytes and stride is 64*4 = 256 bytes, which maps to different banks). Actually, with 32 banks, stride 256 bytes means consecutive threads hit banks (tid * 256 / 4) % 32 = tid * 64 % 32 = 0. NO! All threads hit the same bank! That's a 32-way bank conflict for each access! Oh no. If each thread accesses column c for row tid, and the smem is laid out as [c][tid], then thread 0 accesses [c][0], thread 1 accesses [c][1], etc. The address for thread tid is base + c*64*4 + tid*4. Bank = (address / 4) % 32 = (c*64 + tid) % 32. Wait! (c*64 + tid) % 32 = (tid) % 32 because 64 ≡ 0 (mod 32). So bank = tid % 32. Each thread accesses a different bank! NO bank conflicts! Perfect! Wait, let me recalculate. If smem is float smem[64][64], then &smem[c][tid] = base + (c * 64 + tid) * sizeof(float) = base + (c * 64 + tid) * 4. Bank index = ((c * 64 + tid) * 4 / 4) % 32 = (c * 64 + tid) % 32. Since 64 % 32 = 0, this is tid % 32. So thread 0 accesses bank 0, thread 1 accesses bank 1, ..., thread 31 accesses bank 31. Thread 32 accesses bank 0, etc. So there are exactly 2 threads per bank (since block has 64 threads). But in a single warp (32 threads), each thread accesses a DIFFERENT bank. Zero bank conflicts within a warp! This is because a 64×64 float array with row-major layout, where each row has 64*4 = 256 bytes, and 256 bytes / 32 banks = 8 bytes per bank cycle. Actually, the bank index cycles every 32 elements. Since our row is 64 elements, the second half of threads (32..63) map to the same banks as the first half (0..31). But in a warp, we only have 32 threads, so all 32 access different banks. For 64 threads (2 warps), warp 0 accesses banks 0..31, warp 1 accesses banks 0..31. But warps execute independently, so no conflict. This is perfect! The shared memory layout [64][64] with 64 threads each reading a different spatial position for a fixed channel has zero bank conflicts for warp shuffle operations. Wait, but in our kernel, for a fixed spatial position (thread), we need to read all channels. That means thread tid reads smem[0][tid], smem[1][tid], smem[2][tid], etc. For each channel c, the address is base + c*256 + tid*4. All threads in the warp access consecutive addresses? No, for a fixed c: - Thread 0: base + c*256 - Thread 1: base + c*256 + 4 - These are contiguous! But they're contiguous in shared memory, which means they map to different banks. Wait, I calculated that for fixed c and varying tid, banks are tid%32. That's different banks. For varying c and fixed tid (what we want for processing): - Thread tid reads smem[0][tid], smem[1][tid], etc. - Address for c: base + c*256 + tid*4 - Bank: (c*64 + tid) % 32 = tid % 32 (fixed for all c!) So as thread tid iterates over c=0,1,2,..., it always accesses the SAME bank! There's no bank conflict because only one thread is accessing that bank at a time for a given c. Bank conflicts happen when multiple threads in a warp access the same bank simultaneously. Here, thread tid is the ONLY thread accessing bank (tid % 32) for all values of c. So no bank conflicts. This means the shared memory layout is actually perfect for both loading and processing! Here's the kernel design: - Grid: (B, H/8, W/8) - each block handles an 8×8 tile - Block: 64 threads - Shared memory: float tile[64][64] (channels × spatial_positions) Loading phase: ```cuda for (int c = 0; c < 64; c++) { int h = threadIdx.x / 8; int w = threadIdx.x % 8; int global_idx = ((b * 64 + c) * H + h0 + h) * W + w0 + w; tile[c][threadIdx.x] = input[global_idx]; } __syncthreads(); ``` Processing phase (each thread handles one spatial position): ```cuda int tid = threadIdx.x; // Softmax for this position float max_val = -1e30f; for (int c = 0; c < 64; c++) { float val = tile[c][tid] + bias[c]; max_val = fmaxf(max_val, val); } // We need to share max_val among all threads? No, each thread has its OWN position! // With 64 threads and 64 spatial positions, each thread handles exactly one position. // So max is local to each thread. No reduction needed! float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < 64; c++) { sum_exp += expf(tile[c][tid] + bias[c] - max_val); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int c = 0; c < 64; c++) { float val = expf(tile[c][tid] + bias[c] - max_val) * inv_sum; val = tanhf(val) * scaling_factor; tile[c][tid] = val; } __syncthreads(); ``` Wait! Each thread handles a DIFFERENT spatial position. So each thread computes its OWN softmax over channels. There's NO need for inter-thread communication! Each thread loads all 64 channels for its position, computes max, sum, etc., all in registers! No warp shuffle, no shared memory for reduction! The only use of shared memory is to hold the 64×64 tile so that we can coalesce the loads and writes. But actually, do we even need shared memory for that? Let me reconsider. If each thread handles one position and we have 64 threads per block, with 64 threads loading channel by channel: - For c=0, thread 0 loads pos 0, thread 1 loads pos 1, etc. - These loads are NOT coalesced because pos 0 and pos 1 are at global memory locations that are W apart (128 floats = 512 bytes). For a warp of 32 threads, the memory addresses are 0, 512, 1024, 1536, ... bytes apart. This is NOT coalesced. Each load goes to a different cache line (512 / 128 = 4 cache lines apart). Actually, the memory controller can handle this, but it's strided access. For strided access with stride 512 bytes, each thread touches a different cache line. A warp of 32 threads issues 32 cache line requests. The cache line size is 128 bytes. So each request is for 128 bytes. Utilization: each thread reads 4 bytes from a 128-byte line. 3.125% utilization. Is this terrible? For reading 4096 floats, the warp issues 32 * 64 = 2048 loads (over 64 iterations). Actually, each iteration loads a different channel for all 64 positions. In each iteration, a warp of 32 threads loads 32 positions, each from a different cache line. So 32 cache line fetches per warp per channel. Total cache lines fetched: 32 * 64 = 2048 cache lines = 256KB. But actual data read: 4096 floats = 16KB. So we're reading 256KB to get 16KB, a 16x blowup. Total memory read for the whole kernel: 16KB per block. But the memory controller actually moves 256KB. With hundreds of blocks, total memory traffic = 256KB * number_of_blocks. Number of blocks = B * H * W / 64 = 16 * 128 * 128 / 64 = 4096 blocks. Total read traffic = 4096 * 256KB = 1GB. Plus write traffic of similar magnitude = 1GB. Total 2GB at 3.35TB/s = 0.6ms. Without the shared memory transpose (i.e., if we did coalesced access), we'd read 16KB per block * 4096 = 64MB actual data. At 3.35TB/s, that's 0.02ms. But we can't do coalesced access for channel-wise processing without transposing. With the shared memory transpose: - Loading: 16x blowup in memory traffic = 1GB read - Writing: 16x blowup = 1GB write - Total: 2GB, 0.6ms Wait, but we can load coalesced INTO shared memory and write coalesced FROM shared memory. Let me recalculate. For loading into shared memory in coalesced fashion: We need to read the 64×64 tile from global memory. Global memory is (B, C, H, W). For a block at (b, h0, w0), to read channel 0 for all 64 positions in a coalesced way: - The 64 positions are: (h0+0, w0..w0+7), (h0+1, w0..w0+7), ..., (h0+7, w0..w0+7) - For channel 0, memory layout is: (b*C + 0)*H*W + h*W + w - For (h0, w0..w0+7), addresses are contiguous: base + 0, base + 1, ..., base + 7 - For (h0+1, w0..w0+7), addresses are base + W + 0, base + W + 1, ..., base + W + 7 - These are contiguous within each row, but there's a gap of W-8 between rows If W=128, the gap is 120 floats = 480 bytes. So reading all 64 positions for channel 0 requires 64 reads with gaps. The memory controller might merge these into fewer transactions if the gap is small. Actually, with 128-byte cache lines and 4-byte floats: - Row 0, w=0..7: addresses 0..28 bytes. That's one 128-byte cache line (covers 0..127, which includes w=0..31). Actually, it covers the entire cache line. - Row 0, w=0..7 is fully within one cache line. - Row 1, w=0..7 is at offset W*4 = 512 bytes. That's 4 cache lines away. - So each row needs one cache line, total 8 cache lines for 8 rows. - Each cache line is 128 bytes = 32 floats. We only need 8 floats per row. Utilization: 8/32 = 25%. - For 64 threads, 32 threads in a warp read 4 rows each (since 64 positions / 2 warps). Wait no, if we have 64 threads, and each thread reads one position per channel iteration: - In the first iteration (c=0), threads 0..7 read row 0 w=0..7 (cache line 0), threads 8..15 read row 1 w=0..7 (cache line 4), etc. - Each warp of 32 threads reads 4 rows, each row from a different cache line. Total 4 cache lines per warp. - Data used: 32 floats. Data fetched: 4 * 128 = 512 bytes. Utilization: 128 / 512 = 25%. This is not bad! 25% utilization means 4x overhead. For 4096 blocks reading 16KB each, actual memory moved = 64KB per block. Total read = 4096 * 64KB = 256MB. Write = 256MB. Total = 512MB. At 3.35TB/s = 0.15ms! Plus some compute. Wait, 25% utilization is for 32 threads in a warp. If we have 64 threads in a block, the other 32 threads read the remaining 4 rows in the same pattern, also 25% utilization. And for writing back, same pattern. So total memory traffic is ~512MB, taking ~0.15ms. This is without shared memory! If each thread just handles one position and iterates over channels, reading strided with 25% utilization, total time is ~0.15ms + compute overhead. The compute for softmax is trivial (64 exp per thread). So total kernel time maybe ~0.2ms. With shared memory, we'd have the same loading pattern (25% utilization), but we'd need an extra sync and the write would also be 25% utilization. Total memory traffic is the same (~512MB). Shared memory adds a tiny bit of latency but allows for better data reuse. In our case, each value is only read once and written once, so shared memory doesn't help with reuse. The main benefit would be if the processing required multiple passes or if we wanted to use shared memory for communication between threads. For our per-thread softmax, there's no inter-thread communication needed! So shared memory is actually unnecessary. We can just have each thread read its 64 channels directly from global memory. BUT THERE'S A BUG IN MY 25% CALCULATION: For a block handling 8x8 spatial (64 positions): - If each thread handles one position, thread index maps to (h, w) within the tile. - For reading channel c, thread tid accesses input[b, c, h0 + h, w0 + w]. - A warp of 32 threads: say tid = 0..31. These are (0,0..7), (1,0..7), (2,0..7), (3,0..7). - Thread 0 accesses w=0 of row h=0. - Thread 1 accesses w=1 of row h=0. - ... - Thread 7 accesses w=7 of row h=0. - These 8 accesses are contiguous in memory (addresses differ by 4 bytes). They fit in one 128-byte cache line. - Thread 8 accesses w=0 of row h=1. This is W floats away = 128*4 = 512 bytes. - This is in a different cache line (line 4 if line 0 contains bytes 0..127). - Similarly, threads 16..23 and 24..31 access rows 2 and 3. So a warp of 32 threads accesses 4 cache lines (one per row). Data fetched: 4 * 128 = 512 bytes. Data used: 32 * 4 = 128 bytes. Utilization: 25%. But wait, modern GPUs have a memory transaction size of 32 bytes, not 128! On H100 (Hopper), the L1 cache line is 128 bytes, but the memory transaction size (what goes to L2/DRAM) can be 32, 64, or 128 bytes. When a warp requests scattered 32-bit accesses, the memory coalescing unit groups them into the smallest number of 32-byte segments. For 8 contiguous floats = 32 bytes, that's one 32-byte segment. For 4 rows: 4 segments = 128 bytes total. Data used: 128 bytes. Utilization: 100% for 32-byte transactions! Wait, is that right? If the memory transaction size is 32 bytes, then: - Threads 0..7: 8 contiguous floats = 32 bytes → 1 transaction - Threads 8..15: row 1, 8 contiguous floats = 32 bytes → 1 transaction - Threads 16..23: row 2, 1 transaction - Threads 24..31: row 3, 1 transaction Total: 4 transactions of 32 bytes each = 128 bytes of transactions. Data used = 128 bytes. 100% utilization! THIS IS KEY! On modern GPUs (since Volta/Ampere), the memory transaction size for L2 is 32 bytes. So loading 8 contiguous floats from a row is perfectly efficient (32 bytes). Each row in our 8x8 tile needs exactly one 32-byte transaction for the 8 w positions. For 64 threads (2 warps), warp 0 handles rows 0-3, warp 1 handles rows 4-7. Each warp does 4 transactions per channel. Total transactions per block per channel: 8 transactions * 32 bytes = 256 bytes. For 64 channels: 256 * 64 = 16,384 bytes = 16KB. This is exactly the data size! Zero wasted bandwidth! OH MY GOD. This is amazing. So strided access across H dimension with small contiguous W segments is actually perfectly coalesced on modern GPUs as long as each contiguous segment is >= 32 bytes (8 floats for fp32). Our W tile is 8, so exactly 32 bytes! PERFECT! Wait, but what if W tile was 4? Then 4 floats = 16 bytes, would need 2 rows per 32-byte transaction, but if they're 512 bytes apart, that's impossible. So 8 is perfect. Actually, if we use 16 threads per row (W tile = 16), that's 64 bytes, which is also perfectly fine. Or W tile = 32 (128 bytes), also fine. So the optimal tile width is a multiple of 8 for fp32. For our 8x8 tile: - Each channel read: 8 rows * 1 transaction per row = 8 transactions of 32 bytes = 256 bytes of transactions. - Actual data: 64 floats * 4 bytes = 256 bytes. - Perfect utilization! This means we don't need shared memory at all! We can have each thread handle one position, and the memory loads/writes are perfectly coalesced! But wait, for loading, the threads access memory in the order: - All threads read channel 0 for their positions - Then channel 1 - etc. For each channel, memory access is: - Warp 0 (threads 0..31): rows 0,1,2,3. Each row is a contiguous 32-byte segment. But are these segments adjacent in the L2 cache? They are 512 bytes apart in memory. The memory controller can issue 4 separate 32-byte transactions. This is fine. For H100, each warp can issue up to 32 memory instructions per clock, but more practically, the L2 cache can handle multiple sectors per cycle. The throughput for this pattern should be near the full memory bandwidth for large enough tiles. For writing, the output is at (B, C, 1, H, W). But in practice, contiguous output for a fixed b, c, h has w as the fastest dimension. Same layout! So writes are also perfectly coalesced. WAIT, NO! The output shape is (B, C, 1, H, W). The stride for a 5D tensor is (C*1*H*W, 1*H*W, H*W, W, 1). So for fixed b, c, h, and d=0, the w dimension is contiguous! Yes! Same as the 4D case. Perfect! So here's the final kernel design that doesn't even need shared memory: Kernel configuration: - Each block: 64 threads - Each block handles: 8×8 = 64 spatial positions - Grid: (B, H/8, W/8) = (16, 16, 16) = 4096 blocks Each thread: 1. Computes its (h, w) within the tile 2. Reads 64 channels for its position (one channel at a time, iterative) 3. Finds max 4. Computes sum of exp 5. Normalizes, applies tanh and scale 6. Writes 64 channels back Since B=16, H=128, W=128: - Total positions = 262,144 - Positions per block = 64 - Total blocks = 4096 Wait, can we have 4096 blocks of 64 threads? Yes. Each block processes 64 positions * 64 channels * 4 bytes = 16KB of data. With ~31 blocks per SM, each SM processes ~500KB. At 3.35TB/s shared by 132 SMs, each SM gets ~25GB/s. For 500KB, that's ~20us per SM. Since all SMs run concurrently, total kernel time ~20us! This assumes perfect memory utilization and ignores overhead, but it's clear the kernel will be very fast. Each iteration loads one float per thread. For a warp, 32 threads load 32 floats from 4 different cache lines (one per row). Since each load is to a different cache line, and there are 32 loads, this should be coalesced well. Actually, I want to be even more precise about how the loads are issued. In a warp, threads are executed in lockstep. All threads in a warp execute the same instruction. When they hit a load, the memory coalescing unit looks at all 32 addresses. It groups them into cache lines/segments and issues transactions. For warp 0, threads 0..31 access: - Threads 0..7: row 0, w=0..7 (contiguous, 32 bytes) - Threads 8..15: row 1, w=0..7 (contiguous, 32 bytes, 512 bytes from row 0) - Threads 16..23: row 2, w=0..7 - Threads 24..31: row 3, w=0..7 The addresses span 4 distinct 32-byte segments. The memory unit can issue 4 separate 32-byte transactions. This is well within the capability of the L2 cache on H100. For warp 1, same pattern but rows 4..7. And since we have 64 threads, 2 warps. Both warps execute independently, so there can be up to 8 concurrent 32-byte transactions per block on their way to L2. But the memory subsystem can handle this. Here's the complete kernel plan: - Input: conv2d output (B, C_out, H, W), shape (16, 64, 128, 128) - Bias: (C_out,) - Output: (B, C_out, 1, H, W) - Scaling factor: scalar CUDA kernel: ```cuda __global__ void fused_postprocess( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, float scaling_factor ) { // Block handles 8x8 tile int tw = 8; // tile width int th = 8; // tile height int b = blockIdx.x; int h0 = blockIdx.y * th; int w0 = blockIdx.z * tw; int tid = threadIdx.x; int h = h0 + tid / tw; int w = w0 + tid % tw; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; int spatial_stride = H * W; int base = ((b * C) * H + h) * W + w; // Compute max float max_val = -1e30f; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float val = input[base + c * spatial_stride] + bias[c]; max_val = fmaxf(max_val, val); } // Compute sum of exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum_exp += expf(input[base + c * spatial_stride] + bias[c] - max_val); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Write output for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float val = expf(input[base + c * spatial_stride] + bias[c] - max_val) * inv_sum; val = tanhf(val) * scaling_factor; int out_base = ((b * C + c) * 1 + h) * W + w; // For (B, C, 1, H, W) // Actually: idx = b*C*1*H*W + c*1*H*W + 0*H*W + h*W + w = (b*C + c)*H*W + h*W + w int out_idx = (b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx] = val; } } ``` Wait, I need to double check the output indexing. Now let me write the final code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn import torch.nn.functional as F from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void fused_postprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C, int H, int W, float scaling_factor ) { const int tw = 8; // tile width const int th = 8; // tile height const int tile_size = tw * th; // 64 int b = blockIdx.x; int h0 = blockIdx.y * th; int w0 = blockIdx.z * tw; int tid = threadIdx.x; int h = h0 + tid / tw; int w = w0 + tid % tw; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; int spatial_stride = H * W; int base = ((b * C) * H + h) * W + w; // First pass: find max float max_val = -1e30f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float val = input[base + c * spatial_stride] + bias[c]; max_val = fmaxf(max_val, val); } // Second pass: compute sum of exp float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { sum_exp += expf(input[base + c * spatial_stride] + bias[c] - max_val); } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; // Third pass: normalize, tanh, scale, and write #pragma unroll 4 for (int c = 0; c < C; c++) { float val = expf(input[base + c * spatial_stride] + bias[c] - max_val) * inv_sum; val = tanhf(val) * scaling_factor; int out_idx = (b * C + c) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx] = val; } } torch::Tensor fused_postprocess(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor bias, float scaling_factor) { // input: (B, C, H, W) // bias: (C,) // output: (B, C, 1, H, W) int B = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); auto output = torch::empty({B, C, 1, H, W}, input.options()); const int tw = 8; const int th = 8; dim3 block(tw * th); // 64 threads dim3 grid(B, (H + th - 1) / th, (W + tw - 1) / tw); fused_postprocess_kernel<<<grid, block>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, H, W, scaling_factor ); return output; } ''' cpp_source = ''' torch::Tensor fused_postprocess(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor bias, float scaling_factor); ''' # Compile the extension custom_cuda = load_inline( name="custom_postprocess", cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["fused_postprocess"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, scaling_factor): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() # Keep for weight compatibility if state dict is copied self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d( in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding ) self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, out_channels, 1, 1, 1)) self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor def forward(self, x): B, C_in, D, H, W = x.shape C_out = self.conv_transpose.out_channels # Compute depth-reduced inputs mean_input = x.mean(dim=2) # (B, C_in, H, W) first_slice = x[:, :, 0, :, :] # (B, C_in, H, W) last_slice = x[:, :, -1, :, :] # (B, C_in, H, W) # Prepare combined input for single conv2d neg_first = -first_slice / D neg_last = -last_slice / D combined_input = torch.cat([mean_input, neg_first, neg_last], dim=1) # (B, 3*C_in, H, W) # Prepare conv2d weight w = self.conv_transpose.weight # (C_in, C_out, 3, 3, 3) # PyTorch ConvTranspose3d: out[d] = sum_k w[:, :, k] * input[d + 1 - k] # For mean over depth: result = conv2d(mean, W_sum) - conv2d(first/D, W_0) - conv2d(last/D, W_2) w_0 = w[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) # (C_out, C_in, 3, 3) w_1 = w[:, :, 1, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) w_2 = w[:, :, 2, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) w_sum = w_0 + w_1 + w_2 conv2d_weight = torch.cat([w_sum, w_0, w_2], dim=1) # (C_out, 3*C_in, 3, 3) # Single conv2d conv_out = F.conv2d(combined_input, conv2d_weight, padding=1) # (B, C_out, H, W) # Compute total bias if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None: total_bias = (self.conv_transpose.bias + self.bias.view(-1)).contiguous() else: total_bias = self.bias.view(-1).contiguous() # Call custom CUDA kernel for post-processing output = custom_cuda.fused_postprocess(conv_out, total_bias, self.scaling_factor) return output ``` Wait, I need to double check: `self.conv_transpose.bias` is of shape (out_channels,) and `self.bias.view(-1)` is also (out_channels,). So `total_bias` is correct. One issue: `w_0 = w[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3)`. The original w is (C_in, C_out, 3, 3, 3). After indexing `[:, :, 0, :, :]`, we get (C_in, C_out, 3, 3). Then `permute(1, 0, 2, 3)` gives (C_out, C_in, 3, 3). This is the correct shape for Conv2d weight. Let me also think about whether the numerical precision is sufficient. The tolerance is 1e-4. Our approach should be exact to within floating point rounding. The main differences are: 1. We compute mean before conv instead of after. This accumulates a slightly different amount of rounding error. 2. We split into 3 conv2d terms that sum up. For random values with mean ~0, std ~1: - Mean of 32 values: rounding error ~1e-7 * sqrt(32) ≈ 6e-7 - Conv2d with 48*9=432 MACs: rounding error ~6e-7 * sqrt(432) ≈ 1e-5 - Reference: conv3d with 16*27=432 MACs per depth, total 432*32=13824 MACs per output element before mean. Rounding error ~1 * sqrt(13824) * 1e-7 ≈ 1e-5. Then mean adds minimal error. The difference between our approach and reference should be around 1e-5 or less, well within 1e-4. But wait, our approach does 3 conv2d terms that sum to the result. Reference does 1 conv3d. The accumulation patterns are different. Could the difference be larger? For a well-conditioned problem (random inputs/weights near 0), the difference should be small. But for adversarial cases, it could be larger. The tolerance is 1e-4, which is quite loose for typical fp32 values of O(1). Actually, let me reconsider the weight indexing for conv transpose. I derived the formula based on the adjoint relationship, but I need to make absolutely sure it matches PyTorch's exact implementation. PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d uses the following relationship with Conv3d: If y = Conv3d(x, w, padding=1, stride=1), then ConvTranspose3d(x, w, padding=1, stride=1) computes z such that <y, z> = <x, Conv3d(z, w, ...)>. But what exactly is z in terms of x and w? In the discrete case with zero padding: Conv3d(x, w)[n] = sum_k w[k] * x[n + k - p] ConvTranspose3d(x, w)[n] = sum_k w[k] * x[n - k + p] Wait, this is what I derived. But let me verify with PyTorch docs. Actually, PyTorch docs say: ConvTranspose3d applies a 3D transposed convolution, sometimes called deconvolution. This module can be seen as the gradient of Conv3d with respect to its input. So yes, my formula should be correct. In fact, for stride=1, ConvTranspose3d is equivalent to Conv3d with the weight flipped along all spatial dimensions. But since the weight is learned, the specific orientation doesn't matter for expressiveness - only that we use the same weight orientation as the reference. Given that the reference also uses PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d, and we're using the exact same weight tensor and the exact same mathematical formula, our result should match. One potential issue: does PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d use `padding` in the same way I assumed? In PyTorch, `padding` in ConvTranspose3d corresponds to the amount of padding that was used in the corresponding Conv3d. So for Conv3d with padding=1, ConvTranspose3d with padding=1 is indeed the adjoint. Actually, I just want to do a final sanity check. For PyTorch ConvTranspose3d(padding=1, stride=1, kernel=3): - Output size: (L - 1) * 1 - 2*1 + 3 = L. Good. - The operation: for an input x and output y, if we define the corresponding Conv3d as `y = F.conv3d(x, w.flip([2,3,4]), padding=1)` (weight flipped), then ConvTranspose3d(x, w, padding=1) should equal `F.conv3d(x, w, padding=1)` with the weight NOT flipped but the indexing "transposed". Hmm, but for transpose convolution with stride=1, some sources say it's equivalent to a regular convolution with the kernel rotated 180 degrees (flipped). This depends on whether the weight is stored in conv or correlation orientation. If Conv3d matrix has weight[k] at column n+k-p, then the transpose has weight[k] at row n+k-p, column n. So output[n] = sum_k weight[k] * input[n+k-p]. Wait, that's the SAME as Conv3d! Actually, the backprop is: dx[m] = sum_n dy[n] * w[m - n + p]. Not quite. Actually, the standard result: the transpose of cross-correlation is convolution with flipped kernel. So ConvTranspose3d should be equivalent to Conv3d with flipped kernel. But in PyTorch, since Conv3d already uses cross-correlation (not convolution), the "transpose" of cross-correlation is... let's see. Cross-correlation: (f ⋆ g)[n] = sum_k f[k] * g[n + k] Convolution: (f * g)[n] = sum_k f[k] * g[n - k] The adjoint of cross-correlation is convolution with the flipped f. So ConvTranspose3d(weight) = Conv3d(weight.flip([...])). For stride=1, padding=1: ConvTranspose3d(x, w, padding=1) = Conv3d(x, w.flip([2,3,4]), padding=1) Is this true? If so, then my formula needs to account for the flip. Actually, I just checked: in PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d is NOT the same as Conv2d with flipped kernel when padding != 0. Actually, in the custom kernel, there's a potential issue with register pressure and loop unrolling. I used `#pragma unroll 4` which tells the compiler to unroll the loop in chunks of 4. With C=64, the loop has 16 iterations of 4. This is reasonable. Alternatively, I could just use `#pragma unroll` without a number, but that might fully unroll 64 iterations, causing code bloat. `unroll 4` is a good compromise. 1) / th, (W + tw - 1) / tw);\n \n fused_postprocess_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(\n input.data_ptr<float>(),\n bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n output.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, C, H, W,\n scaling_factor\n );\n \n return output;\n}\n'''\n\ncpp_source = '''\ntorch::Tensor fused_postprocess(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor bias, float scaling_factor);\n'''\n\ncustom_cuda = load_inline(\n name=\"custom_postprocess\",\n cpp_sources=cpp_source,\n cuda_sources=cuda_source,\n functions=[\"fused_postprocess\"],\n extra_cuda_cflags=[\"-O3\", \"--use_fast_math\"],\n verbose=False\n)\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, scaling_factor):\n super(ModelNew, self).__init__()\n self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose3d(\n in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size,\n stride=stride, padding=padding\n )\n self.bias = nn.Parameter(torch.randn(1, out_channels, 1, 1, 1))\n self.scaling_factor = scaling_factor\n \n def forward(self, x):\n B, C_in, D, H, W = x.shape\n \n mean_input = x.mean(dim=2)\n first_slice = x[:, :, 0, :, :]\n last_slice = x[:, :, -1, :, :]\n \n neg_first = -first_slice / D\n neg_last = -last_slice / D\n combined_input = torch.cat([mean_input, neg_first, neg_last], dim=1)\n \n w = self.conv_transpose.weight\n w_0 = w[:, :, 0, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3)\n w_1 = w[:, :, 1, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3)\n w_2 = w[:, :, 2, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3)\n w_sum = w_0 + w_1 + w_2\n \n conv2d_weight = torch.cat([w_sum, w_0, w_2], dim=1)\n conv_out = F.conv2d(combined_input, conv2d_weight, padding=1)\n \n if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None:\n total_bias = (self.conv_transpose.bias + self.bias.view(-1)).contiguous()\n else:\n total_bias = self.bias.view(-1).contiguous()\n \n output = custom_cuda.fused_postprocess(conv_out, total_bias, self.scaling_factor)\n return output\n" }

Let me step back and think about this more carefully. Actually, let's verify: 1. ConvTranspose3d with mean pooling over depth. 2. We have (B, C_in, D, H, W), convtranspose gives (B, C_out, D', H', W'), then mean over dim=2. For valid conv settings where stride=1, padding=1, kernel=3: D' = D - 1 + 3 - 2*1 = D So output depth = input depth. Now, mean pooling over depth: For each (b, c_out, h, w), the value is average over d of convtranspose_output[b, c_out, d, h, w]. The convtranspose_output[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum over c_in, k_d, k_h, k_w of x[b, c_in, d + k_d - 1, h + k_h - 1, w + k_w - 1] * W[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] + b[c_out] When we average over d, we get: sum_d sum_{c_in, k_d, k_h, k_w} x[b, c_in, d + k_d - 1, h + k_h - 1, w + k_w - 1] * W[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] / D This is equivalent to convolving each depth slice and averaging. This is NOT the same as a 2D conv, because the same weight is applied across different depth positions and then averaged, but different depth positions may have different boundary conditions. However, if we think about it differently: = sum_{c_in, k_d} (1/D * sum_d x[b, c_in, d + k_d - 1, :, :]) * W[c_in, c_out, k_d, :, :] with 2D spatial conv For a fixed k_d, the average over d of x[b, c_in, d + k_d - 1, h, w] is equal to the mean over all valid positions in the depth dimension, which for stride=1, kernel=3, padding=1, and same-sized output: - For k_d=0 (padding left): d ranges from 0 to D-1, so we access x[..., d-1, ...] where d-1 ranges from -1 to D-2. Due to padding, x[..., -1, ...] = 0. So we average x[..., 0:D-2, ...]. - For k_d=1: we access x[..., d, ...], average is x[..., 0:D-1, ...]. - For k_d=2 (padding right): we access x[..., d+1, ...], average is x[..., 1:D, ...]. So the averages for k_d=0, 1, 2 are slightly different - each missing one boundary value! This is because the output depth equals input depth (D), and each depth slice output is a weighted sum of neighboring input depth slices. Now if I use my approach of mean_input + neg_first + neg_last, is that correct? mean_input = (1/D) * sum_d x[..., d, ...] neg_first = -(1/D) * x[..., 0, ...] neg_last = -(1/D) * x[..., -1, ...] For k_d=0: we need average of x[..., 0:D-2, ...] = mean_input - (1/D)*x[..., D-1, ...] - (1/D)*x[..., -1] (which is 0) = mean_input - (1/D)*x[..., D-1, ...] = mean_input + something involving last... wait. For k_d=0: mean_input - last/D For k_d=2: mean_input - first/D So I should: combined_input = [mean_input, -last/D, -first/D] Wait, the conv weights are W[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] for k_d=0,1,2.
