So my original code had the wrong order. Also, I should check carefully whether the conv weight transposition is correct, and whether the bias terms are handled correctly. Actually, let me just write a simpler, more direct kernel. Instead of trying to be clever with the conv reduction (which is tricky to get right), I should: 1. Run the conv_transpose normally (PyTorch has a well-optimized conv) 2. Fuse the remaining operations (mean, add bias, softmax, tanh, scale) into a CUDA kernel This is much safer and likely still gives a good speedup because: - The conv_transpose takes most of the time - But fusing 5+ memory-bound operations into 1 kernel significantly reduces memory bandwidth Actually, the conv_transpose outputs (16, 64, 32, 128, 128) which is quite large. The subsequent operations are all elementwise/ reduction which are memory bandwidth bound. Fusing them eliminates multiple read/writes of the full tensor. Let me take the simpler, safer approach. For the CUDA kernel: - Input: tensor of shape (B, C, H, W) after mean pooling (where H and W are the spatial dims after conv) - Actually, after conv_transpose: (B, C_out, D, H, W) - After mean(dim=2): (B, C_out, 1, H, W) if keepdim=True - But we can fuse mean pooling into the kernel too! So the kernel input can be the conv_transpose output (B, C_out, D, H, W), and we compute: 1. For each (b, c, h, w), compute mean over d: sum_d input[b, c, d, h, w] / D 2. Add bias[c] 3. Compute softmax over c (pass 1: max, pass 2: exp and sum, pass 3: normalize) 4. tanh 5. scale This fuses ALL operations after conv_transpose into one kernel! Let me write this kernel. The key is to be careful about shared memory usage and thread coarsening for the softmax. For each spatial position (b, h, w), we need to process C channels. C=64 in the test case, which is small. We can have each thread block handle one (b, h, w) position, and use 64 threads within the block to process the 64 channels. Then we can use shared memory for reductions (max and sum). But wait, for the mean pooling, each channel needs to sum over D=32 depth values. We can have each thread load multiple depth values. Let me design the kernel: For output position (b, c, 0, h, w): - The mean value is (1/D) * sum_d conv_out[b, c, d, h, w] - If D=32 and C=64, then for a given (b, h, w), we need to read 64*32 = 2048 floats from the conv output. - Each warp can handle this easily. Actually, let me think about the thread structure: - Grid: (B, H, W) - one block per batch and spatial position - Block: C threads (e.g., 64 threads) - Each thread handles one channel c, and loops over d to compute the mean. - Then we can use warp-level primitives for softmax reduction since C=64 fits in 2 warps. Let me write this kernel more carefully: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* conv_out, // (B, C, D, H, W) const float* bias, // (C) float* output, // (B, C, 1, H, W) int B, int C, int D, int H, int W, float scaling_factor ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; int tid = threadIdx.x; int c = tid; // Step 1: Compute mean over depth for this channel float mean_val = 0.0f; int conv_stride = C * D * H * W; int base = b * conv_stride + c * D * H * W + h * W + w; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { mean_val += conv_out[base + d * H * W]; } mean_val /= D; mean_val += bias[c]; // Step 2: Softmax over channels // Warp-level reduction for max float max_val = mean_val; for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } // Broadcast max float global_max = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, 0); // Compute exp(x - max) float exp_val = expf(mean_val - global_max); // Warp-level reduction for sum float sum_val = exp_val; for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_val, offset); } // Broadcast sum float global_sum = __shfl_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_val, 0); // Normalize float soft_val = exp_val / global_sum; // Step 3: tanh and scale float out_val = tanhf(soft_val) * scaling_factor; // Store output if (c < C) { int out_idx = ((b * C + c) * 1 + 0) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, this has a problem. The `__shfl_down_sync` only works within a warp. If C=64, we have 2 warps (assuming 32 threads per warp). We need to reduce across both warps. Also, if C is not a multiple of warpSize, or if C > blockDim.x, we need to handle that. Let me rewrite with proper multi-warp reduction using shared memory: ```cuda __global__ void fused_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; int c = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; extern __shared__ float shared[]; float mean_val = 0.0f; if (c < C) { int base = ((b * C + c) * D + 0) * H * W + h * W + w; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { mean_val += conv_out[base + d * H * W]; } mean_val = mean_val / D + bias[c]; } // Softmax max reduction float max_val = mean_val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { // warp reduce max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { shared[threadIdx.x / 32] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) { max_val = shared[threadIdx.x]; for (int offset = blockDim.x / 64; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) shared[0] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); float global_max = shared[0]; // Exp and sum float exp_val = (c < C) ? expf(mean_val - global_max) : 0.0f; float sum_val = exp_val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_val, offset); } if (threadIdx.x % 32 == 0) { shared[threadIdx.x / 32] = sum_val; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32) { sum_val = shared[threadIdx.x]; for (int offset = blockDim.x / 64; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_val, offset); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) shared[0] = sum_val; } __syncthreads(); float global_sum = shared[0]; if (c < C) { float soft_val = exp_val / global_sum; float out_val = tanhf(soft_val) * scaling_factor; int out_idx = ((b * C + c) * 1) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx] = out_val; } } ``` This is cleaner. But wait - if C=64 and we launch 64 threads, `blockDim.x / 32` = 2, so the second reduction within `if (threadIdx.x < blockDim.x / 32)` is only executed by threads 0 and 1. But `__shfl_down_sync` in a partially full warp might still work... Let me be more careful. Actually, for the inter-warp reduction, if we only have 2 warps, thread 0 can read `shared[0]` and `shared[1]`, compute max, and store back. That's much simpler. Also, I should make the block size a power of 2 that covers C. For C=64, block=64. For general C, block=(C + 31) / 32 * 32. Let me simplify the block reduction: ```cuda // Warp reduce max float max_val = mean_val; for (int offset = warpSize/2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } // Write warp max to shared if ((threadIdx.x & 31) == 0) { shared[threadIdx.x >> 5] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); // Warp 0 reads and reduces if (threadIdx.x < (blockDim.x >> 5)) { max_val = shared[threadIdx.x]; for (int offset = (blockDim.x >> 5) / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) shared[0] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); float global_max = shared[0]; ``` This is clean and robust. Wait, but there's another issue. `blockDim.x >> 5` may be 1, 2, 3, etc. For `blockDim.x = 64`, `blockDim.x >> 5 = 2`. For the second reduction, we'd have threads 0 and 1 trying to do a shuffle. `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, ..., 1)` in a warp with threads 0 and 1 active would have thread 1 pull from thread 2 which is not participating. Since we're using full warp mask, thread 2 would return its own value (because it doesn't match the mask condition? No, all threads in the warp execute but only threads satisfying some condition participate). Actually, `__shfl_down_sync` with only some threads active is tricky. A safer approach for the inter-warp reduction with just 2 warps: ```cuda if ((threadIdx.x & 31) == 0) { shared[threadIdx.x >> 5] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); if (threadIdx.x == 0) { float m = shared[0]; for (int i = 1; i < (blockDim.x >> 5); i++) { m = fmaxf(m, shared[i]); } shared[0] = m; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is much simpler and avoids any warp shuffle issues for small numbers of warps! For 2 warps, a simple loop is fast enough. Let me now also think about whether fusing just the post-processing is enough for speedup. The conv_transpose3d in PyTorch uses cuDNN which is highly optimized. For the test config (in=16, out=64, D=32, H=W=128, kernel=3, stride=1), the conv_transpose output is (16, 64, 32, 128, 128). Actually, wait. Let me reconsider - can we speed up the conv_transpose itself? Since it's a transposed conv with stride=1, it's equivalent to a regular conv! ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=1 is the same as Conv3d with the same parameters (for appropriately transposed weights). Actually no, that's not generally true. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1: - Output size = input_size + (kernel - 1) - 2*padding - For kernel=3, padding=1: output_size = input_size + 2 - 2 = input_size This is equivalent to a Conv3d with kernel=3, padding=1 (same output size), but the weight arrangement is different. Actually, for a transposed convolution with stride=1, it IS equivalent to a regular convolution, just with the weight indices flipped in a specific way. But the key is that both cuDNN ConvTranspose and Conv are highly optimized, so there may not be much to gain by rolling our own conv unless we can fuse the subsequent ops. So the strategy is: keep conv_transpose3d as is (highly optimized), but fuse all subsequent ops into one kernel. The remaining ops are: 1. mean(dim=2) - reduces D=32 dimension 2. add(bias) - elementwise 3. softmax(dim=1) - reduces C=64 dimension 4. tanh - elementwise 5. scale - elementwise All of these are memory bandwidth limited. Fusing them into one kernel eliminates 4-5 full tensor read/write passes, which should give a nice speedup. Let me write the complete code now, with proper compiler path handling. Also let me recalculate the output shapes. The input is (16, 16, 32, 128, 128). ConvTranspose3d with kernel=3, stride=1, padding=1 gives output shape: D_out = (D - 1) * stride + kernel - 2*padding = (32 - 1) * 1 + 3 - 2*1 = 31 + 3 - 2 = 32 H_out = (128 - 1) * 1 + 3 - 2 = 128 W_out = 128 So output is (16, 64, 32, 128, 128). Mean over dim=2 gives (16, 64, 1, 128, 128). Our kernel takes conv_out (16, 64, 32, 128, 128) and produces output (16, 64, 1, 128, 128). Total elements in conv_out: 16 * 64 * 32 * 128 * 128 = 536,870,912 floats = 2.15 GB Total elements in output: 16 * 64 * 1 * 128 * 128 = 16,777,216 floats = 67 MB Without fusion: 1. mean: read 2.15 GB, write 67 MB 2. add bias: read 67 MB, write 67 MB 3. softmax: read 67 MB, write 67 MB (plus reduction) 4. tanh: read 67 MB, write 67 MB 5. scale: read 67 MB, write 67 MB Total memory traffic for post-processing ~ 2.15 GB + 5 * 67 MB ≈ 2.48 GB With fusion: Read conv_out once (2.15 GB), write output once (67 MB) Total ≈ 2.22 GB So fusion saves about 10% of total memory bandwidth. Not huge, but still helps. But actually, conv_transpose3d itself is going to be the bottleneck. The time might be dominated by conv_transpose, and the post-processing might be relatively fast. However, if conv_transpose is 80% of the time, and post-processing is 20%, then optimizing post-processing might only give a small speedup overall. Hmm, but in PyTorch the ops might not be well-fused. Let me think about whether there's an opportunity to optimize the conv_transpose itself. One thing I notice: stride=1, so this is effectively a dense convolution. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, output padding defaults to 0, and the formula for output size is valid. Wait, I should double check the PyTorch ConvTranspose3d output size formula. In PyTorch: output_size = (input_size - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 For stride=1, padding=1, dilation=1, kernel_size=3, output_padding=0: output_size = (input_size - 1) - 2 + 2 + 1 = input_size Yes, same size. Could we replace the ConvTranspose3d with a Conv3d? A ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=1, kernel_size=3 is NOT generally the same as Conv3d with the same parameters. But for this specific case... actually, let me think. In 1D for simplicity: ConvTranspose1d is equivalent to convolving with the flipped kernel (as correlation vs convolution). More precisely, ConvTranspose is the gradient of Conv. For stride=1, ConvTranspose would use the same kernel but arranged differently. Actually, there's a known equivalence: ConvTranspose with stride=1, padding=p is equivalent to Conv with stride=1, padding=k-1-p if you flip the weights appropriately. Wait, not exactly. For Conv3d, the forward pass computes (W * x). The backward w.r.t. input computes (W^T * grad_output), which is what ConvTranspose does. So ConvTranspose uses the weight matrix transposed. But spatially, the weights are just flipped in each dimension. Actually for ConvTranspose with stride=1: y[i] = sum_{k} W[k] * x[i - k + padding] (with appropriate boundary handling) And for Conv with stride=1: y[i] = sum_{k} W[k] * x[i + k - padding] These are the same if we flip W: W_flip[k] = W[k_max - k]. But the padding also needs to be different. Actually, let me just keep the ConvTranspose3d as is. It's going to be well-optimized by cuDNN, and trying to replace it with manual CUDA would be very complex and likely slower. So the plan is: 1. Keep conv_transpose3d 2. Fuse all the remaining ops into one kernel Now, one more thing - can I make the fused kernel faster by using a more efficient layout? The output of conv_transpose is (B, C, D, H, W), which in PyTorch is stored as [B, C, D, H, W] in row-major order with C being the channel dimension. The stride for moving in the H dimension is W, in D dimension is H*W, in C dimension is D*H*W. For the mean computation, for fixed (b, c, h, w), we access elements at d=0,1,...,D-1 with stride H*W. These accesses are STRIDED with stride H*W = 128*128 = 16384. This is VERY bad for memory coalescing! Each thread in a warp accessing different d values would have a stride of 16384, leading to 32 cache lines per warp instead of 1-2 if coalesced. Wait, that's a huge issue. Let me reconsider the thread assignment. If we follow my earlier design where each thread handles one (b, c, h, w) and loops over d, then: - Thread 0 accesses (b, c=0, h, w) for d=0,1,...,D-1 with stride H*W - Thread 1 accesses (b, c=1, h, w) for d=0,1,...,D-1 with stride H*W - etc. Threads 0-31 in a warp are accessing: - d=0: (b, c=0..31, h, w) - these are contiguous in memory (stride 1) - d=1: (b, c=0..31, h, w) with offset H*W Wait, no. The memory layout is: offset = b*C*D*H*W + c*D*H*W + d*H*W + h*W + w For fixed (b, h, w, d), contiguous c values are contiguous in memory! For fixed (b, c, h, w), varying d has stride H*W. If each thread handles one c and loops over d, then at iteration d: - Thread c accesses addr = base + c*D*H*W + d*H*W - Thread c+1 accesses addr = base + (c+1)*D*H*W + d*H*W These are separated by D*H*W, not contiguous! So threads in a warp are NOT coalescing. To coalesce, we should have threads in a warp access contiguous memory. For the conv output tensor, contiguous c values have addresses separated by D*H*W. This is actually coalesced IF D*H*W is a multiple of 128 bytes (32 floats). D*H*W = 32*128*128 = 524288. Each thread reads one float, so 32 threads read 32 floats = 128 bytes. The addresses are base + c*524288 + ... for c = 0, 1, 2, ..., 31. 524288 is divisible by 128 bytes (32 floats), so these addresses align to 128-byte boundaries but are separated by 524288 floats = 2 MB. This is NOT coalesced into a single transaction because the addresses are too far apart. Each thread would generate a separate 128-byte load because the addresses are not within the same 128-byte segment. Wait, the definition of coalescing: if all threads in a warp access addresses within the same 128-byte cache line (or within a few contiguous cache lines), they coalesce. If thread c accesses base + c*524288, then thread 0 accesses X, thread 1 accesses X + 2MB. These are 2MB apart, so definitely NOT in the same cache line. Each thread would need a separate 128-byte memory transaction, resulting in 32*128 bytes = 4096 bytes of memory traffic for just 128 bytes of useful data. This is a 32x waste! This is a HUGE problem. To get coalesced access, we need threads to access adjacent memory locations. For the conv output with shape (B, C, D, H, W), adjacent c values are separated by D*H*W = 524288 floats. That's terrible. To read coalesced, we should have threads access either: 1. Same c, adjacent h or w values 2. Same c and h, adjacent w values 3. Adjacent d values with same c... but d values are separated by H*W = 16384 floats, also huge. Actually, what ARE the strides? For (B, C, D, H, W): - W stride: 1 - H stride: W - D stride: H*W - C stride: D*H*W - B stride: C*D*H*W So the most contiguous access is across W dimension (stride 1), then H (stride W=128). For coalescing, we should have threads in a warp handle adjacent w values, or adjacent (h, w) pairs. So instead of having one thread per (b, c, h, w), we should have: - One block per (b, h_block, w_block) or per (b, h, w) with multiple threads in the block accessing different c values - But then different threads access non-contiguous memory (separated by C*D*H*W for same c, or D*H*W for different c) Wait, for thread c accessing (b, c, d, h, w): addr = b*C*D*H*W + c*D*H*W + d*H*W + h*W + w If c varies, addr varies by D*H*W. These are far apart but if D*H*W is a multiple of 32 and memory is accessed via L1/Tex cache, the L2 cache can handle strided patterns to some extent. On H100, the L2 cache is large, and memory access patterns with large strides may still be bandwidth-limited but not as catastrophically as on older GPUs. Actually on modern GPUs like H100, the memory subsystem has improved significantly. Accesses that are strided but aligned to cache lines may not be as bad. Let me think... For thread c: addr = base + c * 524288 + h*128 + w For 32 threads with c = 0..31: addr = base + h*128 + w + [0, 524288, 2*524288, ..., 31*524288] These are all in different 128-byte cache lines (since 524288 >> 32). Each thread triggers a separate 128-byte load. For 32 threads, we read 32 * 128 bytes = 4096 bytes to get 128 bytes of data. That's definitely bad, but it might still be somewhat pipelined. An alternative approach: instead of having threads access different channels, we could have them access different spatial positions or different depth slices. What if we have: - Each block handles (b, c, h_block, w_block) - Each thread in the block handles one (h, w) position - The block loops over d to compute the sum For fixed (b, c), and threads handling different (h, w): At d=0, thread (h, w) accesses addr = base + h*W + w If threads in a warp handle adjacent (h, w) values, then: - For w varying: addr varies by 1 (coalesced!) - For h varying: addr varies by W=128 (still within 1-2 cache lines if W <= 128) So if we have a warp of 32 threads handling 32 adjacent w values (same h), they access contiguous memory! That's fully coalesced. For 128*128 spatial positions, we'd need 128*128/32 = 512 warps per channel. For C=64 channels, that's 32768 warps per batch. For B=16 batches: 524288 warps total, or 16384 thread blocks if each block has 32 warps (1024 threads). Actually, let me think about this differently. Each (b, c, h, w) position needs to sum over D=32 depth values. If I have T threads in a block, I can assign them to different (h, w) positions within a tile. For example: - Block size: 32 threads (1 warp) - Each block handles one (b, c, h_tile, w_tile) where the tile is 1x32 (h fixed, 32 w values) - Each thread loops over D=32 depth values and sums them up - The 32 threads cooperate for the softmax over C... wait, no, if each block only handles one c, then softmax requires communicating across c. Hmm, the issue is that softmax is over C channels, so we need all C channel values for a given (b, h, w) to be in the same computation unit. Options: 1. Each warp/block computes softmax for one (b, h, w). Load all C channels for that position. But C=64 channels with D=32 each means 2048 floats to load. With coalesced access, this is 2048 * 4 bytes = 8 KB per (b, h, w), which is fine. But can we load them coalesced? For (b, h, w) fixed, varying c: addr varies by D*H*W = 524288. NOT coalesced. For (b, c) fixed, varying (h, w): addr varies by 1 or 128. COALESCED. So the memory layout makes it hard to both coalesce AND do softmax efficiently. Let me think carefully. For Conv3d with stride=1, padding=1: - output_size = floor((input_size + 2*padding - dilation*(kernel-1) - 1) / stride + 1) - = (input_size + 2 - 2 - 1) / 1 + 1 = input_size So output size is the same. For ConvTranspose3d with stride=1, padding=1: - output_size = (input_size - 1)*stride - 2*padding + dilation*(kernel-1) + output_padding + 1 - = input_size - 1 - 2 + 2 + 0 + 1 = input_size Same output size. Now, what's the actual relationship between the operations? A transposed convolution is the gradient of a convolution. For stride=1, if we have a forward conv: y = Conv3d(x, W, padding=1) Then the gradient w.r.t. x for the backward pass would use ConvTranspose3d with the same padding and kernel. But the backward pass uses the weights transposed: for each spatial position, the weight indices are effectively reversed. Actually, the transpose of a Toeplitz matrix representing convolution with kernel [w0, w1, w2] is a transposed convolution with the same kernel [w0, w1, w2], not reversed. Wait, that's not right either. The gradient of conv w.r.t. input involves the transpose of the conv matrix. If the conv matrix has structure where each row picks elements [x_{i-1}, x_i, x_{i+1}] multiplied by [w0, w1, w2], then its transpose would have columns with [w0, w1, w2]. The corresponding operation would indeed be a transposed convolution with weights [w0, w1, w2]. Conversely, a transposed convolution with weights W is equivalent to a regular convolution with weights W_flip, where W_flip[k] = W[k_max - k]. For 3D with kernel_size=3 along each dimension: W_flip[d, h, w] = W[2-d, 2-h, 2-w] So ConvTranspose3d(in, W, padding=1, stride=1) is equivalent to Conv3d(in, W_flip, padding=1, stride=1). If this equivalence holds, I could replace ConvTranspose3d with Conv3d using flipped weights, which should be more efficient in cuDNN (since regular conv is typically faster than transposed conv). Can I avoid the conv_transpose entirely? The model does: 1. ConvTranspose3d 2. Mean over depth 3. Add bias 4. Softmax 5. Tanh 6. Scale What if the mean over depth allows me to collapse the 3D operation into a simpler form? For ConvTranspose3d with kernel=(3,3,3), stride=(1,1,1), padding=(1,1,1): The output at position (d, h, w) is: sum_{c_in, k_d, k_h, k_w} x[c_in, d + k_d - 1, h + k_h - 1, w + k_w - 1] * W[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] + b[c_out] Then mean over d: (1/D) * sum_d sum_{c_in, k_d, k_h, k_w} x[c_in, d + k_d - 1, h + k_h - 1, w + k_w - 1] * W[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] + b[c_out] = sum_{c_in, k_d, k_h, k_w} [(1/D) * sum_d x[c_in, d + k_d - 1, h + k_h - 1, w + k_w - 1]] * W[c_in, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] + b[c_out] For k_d=0: sum_d x[..., d-1, ...] = sum_{d=0}^{D-2} x[..., d, ...] (since padding makes d=-1 zero) For k_d=1: sum_d x[..., d, ...] = sum_{d=0}^{D-1} x[..., d, ...] For k_d=2: sum_d x[..., d+1, ...] = sum_{d=1}^{D-1} x[..., d, ...] So: mean over d of k_d=0: (sum_{d=0}^{D-2} x[d]) / D = mean_all - x[D-1]/D mean over d of k_d=1: (sum_{d=0}^{D-1} x[d]) / D = mean_all mean over d of k_d=2: (sum_{d=1}^{D-1} x[d]) / D = mean_all - x[0]/D So the entire operation is: sum_{c_in, k_h, k_w} mean_all[c_in, h+k_h-1, w+k_w-1] * (W[c_in, c_out, 0, k_h, k_w] + W[c_in, c_out, 1, k_h, k_w] + W[c_in, c_out, 2, k_h, k_w]) - sum_{c_in, k_h, k_w} x[c_in, D-1, h+k_h-1, w+k_w-1]/D * W[c_in, c_out, 0, k_h, k_w] - sum_{c_in, k_h, k_w} x[c_in, 0, h+k_h-1, w+k_w-1]/D * W[c_in, c_out, 2, k_h, k_w] This can be computed as THREE separate 2D convolutions: 1. conv2d(mean_all_input, W_sum) where W_sum = W[:, :, 0, :, :] + W[:, :, 1, :, :] + W[:, :, 2, :, :] 2. conv2d(-last_slice/D, W[:, :, 0, :, :]) 3. conv2d(-first_slice/D, W[:, :, 2, :, :]) Then add results together, add bias, softmax, tanh, scale. This completely avoids the 3D conv_transpose and the mean pooling! It reduces the heavy conv3d operation to three conv2d operations. But is this actually faster? - One 3D conv on (16, 16, 32, 128, 128) → (16, 64, 32, 128, 128) - Or three 2D convs on (16, 16, 128, 128) → (16, 64, 128, 128) 3D conv complexity: B * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out * C_in * K_d * K_h * K_w = 16 * 64 * 32 * 128 * 128 * 16 * 3 * 3 * 3 = 68.7 billion multiply-adds 2D conv complexity for one: B * C_out * H_out * W_out * C_in * K_h * K_w = 16 * 64 * 128 * 128 * 16 * 3 * 3 = 2.39 billion multiply-adds Three 2D convs: 7.17 billion multiply-adds So the 2D approach is ~9.6x fewer operations! This is because we're avoiding the depth dimension (D=32) and exploiting the mean pooling. Wait, but we've lost the 3D nature. Is this actually correct? Let me trace through carefully: Original: y[d, h, w] = sum_{c} sum_{k_d, k_h, k_w} x[c, d+k_d-1, h+k_h-1, w+k_w-1] * W[c, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] mean_y[h, w] = (1/D) sum_d y[d, h, w] = sum_{c} sum_{k_d, k_h, k_w} [(1/D) sum_d x[c, d+k_d-1, h+k_h-1, w+k_w-1]] * W[c, c_out, k_d, k_h, k_w] For k_d=0 (and valid d values 0..D-1): d+k_d-1 = d-1 ranges from -1 to D-2. For d=0, d-1=-1 which is padding=0. For d=1, d-1=0. ... For d=D-1, d-1=D-2. sum_d x[c, d-1, ...] = sum_{d=0}^{D-2} x[c, d, ...] = total_sum - x[c, D-1, ...] For k_d=1: d+k_d-1 = d ranges from 0 to D-1. sum_d x[c, d, ...] = total_sum For k_d=2: d+k_d-1 = d+1 ranges from 1 to D. For d=D-1, d+1=D which is padding=0. sum_d x[c, d+1, ...] = sum_{d=1}^{D-1} x[c, d, ...] = total_sum - x[c, 0, ...] So: mean_y[h, w] = sum_{c} sum_{k_h, k_w} [total_sum - x[c, D-1, ...]]/D * W[c, c_out, 0, k_h, k_w] + sum_{c} sum_{k_h, k_w} [total_sum]/D * W[c, c_out, 1, k_h, k_w] + sum_{c} sum_{k_h, k_w} [total_sum - x[c, 0, ...]]/D * W[c, c_out, 2, k_h, k_w] = conv2d(total_sum/D, W_0 + W_1 + W_2) + conv2d(-x[D-1]/D, W_0) + conv2d(-x[0]/D, W_2) where total_sum/D is the mean of x over depth, and W_k = W[:, :, k, :, :]. Now, one potential issue: `torch.cat` might create a copy of the data, which uses memory bandwidth. Three separate conv2d: - Each has input (B, C_in, H, W) and weight (C_out, C_in, 3, 3) - Total memory for inputs: x_mean + x_first + x_last = 3 * B * C_in * H * W floats - But x_mean is already computed - Each conv reads its input and weight, writes output - Three kernel launches - Plus a fourth kernel to add the three outputs One conv with cat: - One input (B, 3*C_in, H, W) - One weight (C_out, 3*C_in, 3, 3) - One kernel launch - But cat creates a copy (2 * B * 3 * C_in * H * W bytes read/write) For B=16, C_in=16, H=128, W=128: - x_mean = 16*16*128*128 = 4.2M floats = 16.7 MB - cat input = 3 * 16.7 = 50.1 MB - cat operation writes 50.1 MB, reads two slices (no read for x_mean since it's a new tensor, but x_first and x_last are copied into the cat buffer) - conv reads 50.1MB input, writes 16.7MB output Three separate convs: - conv1 reads 16.7MB, writes 16.7MB - conv2 reads 16.7MB, writes 16.7MB - conv3 reads 16.7MB, writes 16.7MB - Total read: 50.1MB, write: 50.1MB - Plus add kernel: read 50.1MB, write 16.7MB Three convs have more total memory traffic (100.2MB read + 50.1MB write + 16.7MB write for add = 167MB) vs cat approach (50.1MB for cat + 50.1MB for conv read + 16.7MB output = 116.9MB). Also, for the fused kernel, since we only need softmax+tanh+scale now (bias is handled by conv2d), the kernel is simpler. Kernel signature: ```cpp torch::Tensor fused_postprocess(torch::Tensor input, float scaling_factor); ``` Where input is (B, C, H, W) and output is (B, C, 1, H, W). For each (b, h, w), we compute softmax over c, then tanh, then scale. Kernel: ```cuda __global__ void postprocess_kernel( const float* input, float* output, int B, int C, int H, int W, float scaling_factor ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int w = blockIdx.z; if (b >= B || h >= H || w >= W) return; extern __shared__ float shared[]; int tid = threadIdx.x; // Load input values for this (b, h, w) float val = 0.0f; if (tid < C) { int idx = ((b * C) + tid) * H * W + h * W + w; val = input[idx]; } // Softmax max reduction float max_val = val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } if ((tid & 31) == 0) { shared[tid >> 5] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < (blockDim.x >> 5)) { max_val = shared[tid]; for (int offset = (blockDim.x >> 5) / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, max_val, offset)); } if (tid == 0) shared[0] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); float global_max = shared[0]; // Compute exp and sum float exp_val = (tid < C) ? expf(val - global_max) : 0.0f; float sum_val = exp_val; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_val, offset); } if ((tid & 31) == 0) { shared[tid >> 5] = sum_val; } __syncthreads(); if (tid < (blockDim.x >> 5)) { sum_val = shared[tid]; for (int offset = (blockDim.x >> 5) / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, sum_val, offset)); } if (tid == 0) shared[0] = sum_val; } __syncthreads(); float global_sum = shared[0]; if (tid < C) { float soft = exp_val / global_sum; float out_val = tanhf(soft) * scaling_factor; int out_idx = ((b * C) + tid) * H * W + h * W + w; output[out_idx] = out_val; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with the inter-warp shuffle when blockDim.x >> 5 is not a power of 2 or is small. For C=64, blockDim.x could be 64, so blockDim.x >> 5 = 2. Only threads 0 and 1 enter the `if (tid < 2)` block. Then `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, ..., 1)` would have thread 0 read from thread 1 and thread 1 read from thread 2. Thread 2 is in the same warp but not participating. On modern GPUs, inactive threads in a shuffle return their own value when using the full mask. So thread 0 gets shared[1], thread 1 gets thread 1's own value (which is shared[1]). So max_val = fmaxf(shared[0], shared[1]) for thread 0, and max_val = fmaxf(shared[1], shared[1]) = shared[1] for thread 1. Then we write shared[0] only from thread 0. This works! But let me make it even simpler. For the inter-warp reduction with just 2 warps, a simple loop is clearer: ```cuda if ((tid & 31) == 0) { shared[tid >> 5] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); if (tid == 0) { float m = shared[0]; for (int i = 1; i < (blockDim.x >> 5); i++) { m = fmaxf(m, shared[i]); } shared[0] = m; } __syncthreads(); ``` This is simple and correct for any number of warps. Also, I need to allocate shared memory. For 2 warps, I need 2 floats. To be safe, I can allocate 32 floats or compute it dynamically: ```cuda extern __shared__ float shared[]; ``` And in the launch: `postprocess_kernel<<<grid, block, num_warps * sizeof(float)>>>` OK let me now write the complete Python code. I'll need to carefully handle all the shape calculations and make sure the output matches exactly. I can then extract it and use it for my combined_weight. Let me verify that my math is perfectly correct by comparing outputs for a small random example before compiling. Actually, I can't run Python code here, but I can reason through it carefully. The key identity: mean(ConvTranspose3d(x, W, padding=1), dim=2) = conv2d(mean(x, dim=2), W_sum.permute(1,0,2,3)) + conv2d(-x_last/D, W_0.permute(1,0,2,3)) + conv2d(-x_first/D, W_2.permute(1,0,2,3)) where W_k = W[:, :, k, :, :] and the conv2d weights need to be transposed from (C_in, C_out, ...) to (C_out, C_in, ...). Wait, I need to double-check the weight transposition. The ConvTranspose3d weight W has shape (C_in, C_out, K_d, K_h, K_w). When we do ConvTranspose3d(x, W), the output for channel c_out is computed by summing over c_in and all kernel positions. In terms of 2D conv for fixed depth slice, it's like using W[:, c_out, k_d, :, :] as the 2D kernel for that c_out. In standard Conv2d, the weight has shape (C_out, C_in, K_h, K_w). For output channel c_out, the 2D kernel is weight[c_out, :, :, :]. So if I want to use F.conv2d, I need the 2D kernel for c_out to be W[:, c_out, k_d, :, :] transposed to (c_out, :, :, :). W[:, :, k_d, :, :] has shape (C_in, C_out, K_h, K_w). To get (C_out, C_in, K_h, K_w), I do permute(1, 0, 2, 3). So W_k_permuted = W[:, :, k_d, :, :].permute(1, 0, 2, 3) Yes, that's correct. And W_sum_permuted = W_0_permuted + W_1_permuted + W_2_permuted = (W[:, :, 0, :, :] + W[:, :, 1, :, :] + W[:, :, 2, :, :]).permute(1, 0, 2, 3) Since permute is linear, this is the same as permuting then adding. So my weight computation is correct. Now let me think about whether there could be numerical differences with the reference. The main potential issue is the mean. PyTorch's mean uses Welford's algorithm or a simple sum for well-conditioned cases. For D=32 and values in [0, 1], the simple sum / D should be numerically stable and produce the same result. For the conv, F.conv2d uses cuDNN which may have slightly different accumulation order than ConvTranspose3d. But since we're comparing to a model that also uses ConvTranspose3d with its own accumulation, and cuDNN uses similar algorithms, the difference should be within tolerance (1e-4 for fp32). Actually, wait. My derived formula replaces a transposed conv with regular convs. Are the accumulation orders the same? Probably not exactly, but for fp32 with values in [0, 1] and kernel size 3, the difference should be very small. But there's also the issue of padding. Does PyTorch ConvTranspose3d with padding=1 pad with zeros exactly as expected? Yes. Let me also verify that for the first and last slices, the padding zeros are handled correctly in the mean. For the last slice x[..., D-1, ...], in the conv output it's used for k_d=0 as: y[d=D-1, h, w] includes x[..., D-2, ...] * W[..., 0, ...] + x[..., D-1, ...] * W[..., 1, ...] + x[..., D, ...] * W[..., 2, ...] where x[..., D, ...] is padding=0. So the contribution to the mean from k_d=2 using x[..., D, ...] is 0, which is correct since x[..., D, ...] doesn't exist and is zero-padded. In my formula: mean over d for k_d=2 uses sum_d x[..., d+1, ...] for d=0..D-1. = x[..., 1, ...] + ... + x[..., D, ...] where x[..., D, ...] = 0. = total_sum - x[..., 0, ...] So mean = (total_sum - x[..., 0, ...]) / D = mean_all - x[..., 0, ...]/D And the weight for this term is W_2. Similarly for k_d=0: sum_d x[..., d-1, ...] = x[..., -1, ...] + x[..., 0, ...] + ... + x[..., D-2, ...] where x[..., -1, ...] = 0. = total_sum - x[..., D-1, ...] Mean = (total_sum - x[..., D-1, ...]) / D = mean_all - x[..., D-1, ...]/D Weight for this term is W_0. And for k_d=1: sum_d x[..., d, ...] = total_sum Mean = total_sum / D = mean_all Weight is W_1. So: final_mean = conv2d(mean_all, W_0 + W_1 + W_2) + conv2d(-x_last/D, W_0) + conv2d(-x_first/D, W_2) = conv2d(mean_all, W_sum) + conv2d(-x_last/D, W_0) + conv2d(-x_first/D, W_2) This is correct. For the postprocess kernel, since the input after conv2d is (B, C, H, W), and output should be (B, C, 1, H, W): ```python torch::Tensor fused_postprocess(torch::Tensor input, float scaling_factor) { int B = input.size(0); int C = input.size(1); int H = input.size(2); int W = input.size(3); auto output = torch::empty({B, C, 1, H, W}, input.options()); dim3 grid(B, H, W); int block_size = ((C + 31) / 32) * 32; if (block_size > 1024) block_size = 1024; dim3 block(block_size); int shared_size = (block_size / 32) * sizeof(float); if (shared_size < 2 * sizeof(float)) shared_size = 2 * sizeof(float); postprocess_kernel<<<grid, block, shared_size>>>( input.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, C, H, W, scaling_factor ); return output; } ``` Wait, output needs to have the right shape and layout. In PyTorch, `channels_last` is a memory format. For 2D conv, `torch.channels_last` corresponds to NHWC. If I make the conv2d output channels_last, then the tensor layout is (B, H, W, C) in memory. But PyTorch still reports the shape as (B, C, H, W). Wait, in PyTorch channels_last, the semantic shape is still (N, C, H, W), but the storage is in NHWC order. The strides are (C*H*W, 1, W*C, C) or something like that. Let me think... For channels_last (NHWC): - stride_N = H*W*C - stride_C = 1 - stride_H = W*C - stride_W = C Actually no. In PyTorch channels_last for 2D, the strides are: - stride[N] = H * W * C if contiguous in NHWC - stride[C] = 1 - stride[H] = W * C - stride[W] = C Wait, PyTorch's channels_last is actually more complex. The tensor reports shape (N, C, H, W) but with strides that make it NHWC-like. For a channels_last tensor of shape (N, C, H, W): - stride[0] = C * H * W if single batch, but actually... Let me check: `torch.empty(N, C, H, W).to(memory_format=torch.channels_last).stride()` This gives `(C*H*W, 1, W*C, C)` approximately... no, let's think. Actually for channels_last: - The last dimension in memory is channel - So for index [n, c, h, w], the memory offset is n*H*W*C + h*W*C + w*C + c - stride = (H*W*C, 1, W*C, C) Hmm, wait. The stride for dimension i is the number of elements to skip when incrementing index[i] by 1. For [n, c, h, w] with memory layout NHWC: - Increment n by 1: skip H*W*C elements - Increment c by 1: skip 1 element - Increment h by 1: skip W*C elements - Increment w by 1: skip C elements So stride = (H*W*C, 1, W*C, C). Yes. Then for thread c accessing (b, c, h, w): addr = base + c These are CONTIGUOUS across c! COALESCED! For the post-process kernel, if we get channels_last input, then threads 0..31 accessing c=0..31 at same (b, h, w) are fully coalesced. This is MUCH better. Can F.conv2d produce channels_last output? Yes, if the input is channels_last and the weights are also channels_last, the output will be channels_last. So I should convert everything to channels_last: 1. x_mean, x_first, x_last should be channels_last 2. The weight should be channels_last 3. F.conv2d should output channels_last 4. My post-process kernel should handle channels_last input For step 4, if the input is channels_last, the indexing changes. But PyTorch keeps the semantic shape as (N, C, H, W). The underlying memory is different. In my CUDA kernel, if I use `input.data_ptr<float>()` and compute offsets manually, I need to know the actual strides. But for a contiguous channels_last tensor of shape (N, C, H, W), I can compute offsets as: offset = n*H*W*C + h*W*C + w*C + c This is easy! And much better for coalescing. However, I need to be careful: `input.is_contiguous(memory_format=torch.channels_last)` or I can just query the strides. Or, if I want to keep it simple, I can just use the tensor's `.stride()` information in the kernel. But passing strides to the kernel adds complexity. A simpler approach: in the Python code, after conv2d, I can permute the tensor from NCHW to NHWC using `.permute(0, 2, 3, 1).contiguous()`, run my kernel on NHWC layout, then permute back to NCHW. But the permute operations cost memory bandwidth. Actually, for (B, C, H, W) = (16, 64, 128, 128): - Permute read + write = 2 * 16*64*128*128 * 4 bytes = 134 MB - Post-process kernel read + write = 67 MB + 67 MB = 134 MB Total = 268 MB Without permute, but with poor coalescing: - Post-process kernel read + write = effectively 134 MB * amplification_factor If the amplification factor is 2-4x due to bad coalescing, permute might actually be faster! But actually, on H100, the L2 cache is large and the memory controllers are smart. NCHW access with warp-stride of H*W might not be as bad as 32x amplification. Each warp of 32 threads accessing channels 0..31 would generate 32 separate 128-byte reads (since each thread's address is in a different 128-byte segment). That's 4096 bytes read for 128 bytes of data. But the L1 cache might still retain some of these for subsequent warps. This is tricky. Let me just use channels_last for the conv2d output and adapt my kernel. To make the conv2d produce channels_last output, I need: ```python x_mean_cl = x_mean.to(memory_format=torch.channels_last) x_first_cl = x_first.to(memory_format=torch.channels_last) x_last_cl = x_last.to(memory_format=torch.channels_last) concat_input = torch.cat([x_mean_cl, -x_last_cl / D, -x_first_cl / D], dim=1) # But wait, channels_last cat might preserve the format. # And the weight should also be channels_last combined_weight_cl = self.combined_weight.to(memory_format=torch.channels_last) conv_out = F.conv2d(concat_input, combined_weight_cl, bias=self.combined_bias, padding=1) ``` If concat_input is channels_last and combined_weight_cl is channels_last, F.conv2d will output channels_last. But torch.cat on channels_last tensors... does it preserve channels_last? I think so, as long as the cat is along the channel dimension. Hmm, this is getting complex. Let me just try the simplest approach first: NCHW throughout, simple kernel. If performance isn't good enough, I'll add channels_last. Actually, I just realized I should try to avoid the post-process kernel's coalescing issue by assigning threads differently. What if I assign one warp to handle 32 spatial positions (same c, different w or h), and they do reduction across c using shared memory? But then each warp would need all C values for each of its 32 spatial positions... no, that doesn't work because softmax is per spatial position. Actually, what if I process multiple spatial positions simultaneously in a block, but all positions share the same softmax computation? No, softmax is per position. OK, for the post-process, the block assignment is inherently one block per (b, h, w) because softmax is across c for each spatial position. And within that block, threads access different c values. The only way to improve coalescing is to use channels_last. But wait - I just realized something. The output of conv2d with NCHW layout and C=64, H=128, W=128 has C_stride = 16384. When we have 64 threads accessing c=0..63 at the same (b, h, w), they're separated by 16384 floats. 16384 floats * 4 bytes = 65536 bytes = 64 KB. On H100, the L1 cache per SM is 256 KB shared between L1 and shared memory. The L2 cache is much larger. A 64 KB stride means that c=0 and c=1 data are 64 KB apart. If a warp reads c=0..31 at (b, h, w), that's 32 floats = 128 bytes. But each float is in a different 64 KB region. The warp needs 32 cache lines. On H100, a warp can issue up to 32 memory requests. Each subsequent warp in the same SM (processing adjacent h or w) will access data that is spatially nearby. For (b, h, w+1), thread c accesses base + c*16384 + 1. This is in the same 64 KB region as the previous warp, just offset by 1. If the L1 cache is 256 KB, it can hold about 4 channels worth of data for one row. So there's some L1 reuse across warps processing adjacent w values. Actually, wait. For c=0, (h, w) and (h, w+1) are adjacent in memory (stride 1). For c=1, they're also adjacent. So when block (b, h, w) and block (b, h, w+1) run on the same SM, their accesses for c=0 are adjacent, for c=1 are adjacent, etc. But the L1 cache stores data by address. For block (b, h, w), it loads c=0 data at address A, c=1 data at address A+64KB, etc. For block (b, h, w+1), it loads c=0 data at address A+1, c=1 data at address A+64KB+1, etc. The L1 cache line is 128 bytes. So address A and A+1 are in the same cache line. But A and A+64KB are not. When the next block accesses A+1 and A+64KB+1, the A+1 part might already be in cache if the previous block loaded the cache line containing A..A+127. But A+64KB+1 is a completely different cache line. So there's some spatial locality across adjacent w values for each channel individually, but not across channels. Hmm. I think for practical purposes, the H100's massive memory bandwidth (3.35 TB/s) can probably handle this pattern at several hundred GB/s effective. And since the post-process is much cheaper than the conv we replaced, the overall speedup should still be huge. Let me just implement it and see. The 3D→2D conv optimization alone should provide most of the speedup. One final thought: what if I fuse the softmax, tanh, scale INTO the conv2d using a custom cuDNN epilogue? PyTorch doesn't expose this easily, so probably not worth it. OK, I'm going with this approach. Let me write the complete code now.

callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "import torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.nn.functional as F\nimport os\nimport shutil\nfrom torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline\n\n# Set up C++ os.path.exists(cxx):\n os.environ['CXX'] = cxx\n\ncuda_source = '''\n#include <torch/extension.h>\n#include <cuda_runtime.h>\n#include <math.h>\n\n__global__ void postprocess_kernel(\n const float* reasoning
The issue is with the 3D transposed convolution followed by mean pooling. Let me trace this more carefully. For ConvTranspose3d with: - in_channels = 16, out_channels = 64 - kernel_size = 3, stride = 1, padding = 1 - Weight shape: (16, 64, 3, 3, 3) The output of ConvTranspose3d is (B, 64, D, H, W) = (B, 64, 32, 128, 128). Then mean over depth gives (B, 64, 1, 128, 128). For a given output position (d, h, w) in the ConvTranspose3d output: y[b, c_out, d, h, w] = sum over c_in, kd, kh, kw of x[b, c_in, d+kd-1, h+kh-1, w+kw-1] * w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] where d ranges from -padding to D + padding - 1 = 0 to 30 (D=32, padding=1). Mean over depth: mean_y[b, c_out, h, w] = (1/32) * sum_{d=0}^{31} y[b, c_out, d, h, w] For each d in [0, 31], the ConvTranspose3d reads from input depth d+kd-1 for kd in {0,1,2}. For a given input slice xi = x[b, c_in, i, h, w] (where i ranges from 0 to 31): - d=0: uses i=-1,0,1 -> only i=0,1 contribute (boundary) - d=0,1: ... - d=1,2,...,30: uses i=d-1,d,d+1 -> full range - d=31: uses i=30,31,32 -> only i=30,31 contribute (boundary) Actually for the interior, xi is used for d=i+1 (with kd=0), d=i (with kd=1), d=i-1 (with kd=2). Wait: for output position d and kernel index kd, the input index is d + kd - 1 (with stride=1). - kd=0 -> input index = d-1 - kd=1 -> input index = d - kd=2 -> input index = d+1 For xi to contribute to y[d] with kernel weight w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw]: xi contributes via: - kd=0: d=i+1 -> xi appears in y[i+1] if i+1 < D_out - kd=1: d=i -> xi appears in y[i] - kd=2: d=i-1 -> xi appears in y[i-1] if i-1 >= 0 So total contribution coefficients of xi to the mean: coef_i = w[:,:,0,:,:]/D for interior (i=1,...,D-2) via kd=0 at d=i+1 + w[:,:,1,:,:]/D for interior via kd=1 at d=i + w[:,:,2,:,:]/D for interior via kd=2 at d=i-1 For boundary i=0: only kd=1 and kd=2 contribute, d=0 uses di=0,1 For boundary i=D-1: only kd=0 and kd=1 contribute So: mean(conv3d(x))[d_in=0] uses: x[0]*(w1 + w2)/D + x[1]*w2/D mean(conv3d(x))[d_in=0] = Actually, at position d=0 of output: input read: d=0+kd-1 for kd=0,1,2: - input_d = -1, 0, 1 -> clipped to 0, 0, 1 So: x[:,:,0] * (w0 + w1) + x[:,:,1] * w2 For d from 1 to D-2: input_d = d-1, d, d+1 -> all valid Contribution of x[:,:,i] to mean: For d=i-1: w2/D For d=i: w1/D For d=i+1: w0/D Total: (w0 + w1 + w2)/D For d=D-1: input_d = D-2, D-1, D -> clipped to D-2, D-1, D-1 Contribution: x[:,:,D-2] * w0 + x[:,:,D-1] * (w1 + w2) But we're summing over d from 0 to D: mean = (1/D) * sum over output positions For x[:,:,0]: contributes to d=0 with w0+w1 (no clipping), d=-1 with w2 (clipped) For x[:,:,1]: contributes to d=0 (w2), d=1 (w1), d=2 (w0) mean coeff of x[:,:,0]: (w0 + w1)/D mean coeff of x[:,:,1]: (w0 + w1 + w2)/D mean coeff of x[:,:,2]: (w0 + w1 + w2)/D ... mean coeff of x[:,:,D_last=D-1]: (w1 + w2)/D Wait, actually for d=0, the input depths used are: kd=0: d-1=-1 (clipped or padded with 0 since no explicit padding in TransposedConv - actually it computes for d=0 to D_out-1).
